6 min
Types of I'timad and Critique of the Six-Type Division
Al-Taftazani critiques the six-type division of i'timad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Taftazani criticizes the six-type classification of i'timad based on directions, and why only upward and downward are considered natural and real.

Core Idea
Only upward and downward i'timad are natural; other directional types are conventional and not genuinely distinct.

After defining i'timad, al-Taftazani turns to its classification according to directions. He presents the view that there are six types corresponding to the six directions, but then systematically critiques this division. He argues that natural i'timad is only upward and downward because these are the only real, unchanging directions. The other four directions (front, back, right, left) are relative and changeable, so i'timad associated with them is not a distinct type. Moreover, the enumeration of six directions itself is merely conventional, not real.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how al-Taftazani first states the six-type view, then provides two criticisms.
The critique is detailed; read carefully.
2
Understand the first weakness
Only upward and downward are real directions because they do not change with orientation. Even if a person is inverted, up remains up and down remains down. Other directions (front, back, etc.) are relative and change, so their corresponding i'timad are not stable types.
The example of a person turning around illustrates the relativity.
3
Understand the second weakness
The sixfold division of directions is conventional, based on the human body (head, feet, back, belly, right, left). Specialists recognize that directions are more numerous or even infinite, so the division is not real.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Natural i'timad is only heaviness (thiqal) and lightness (khiffa), corresponding to downward and upward respectively. The so-called six types do not represent genuine differences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : المبحث الثاني: من الملموسات الاعتماد فيمن يجعله نفس المدافعة المحسوسة لما يمنع الحركة إلى جهة ما لا مبدأها المعقول . قال : قد يراد بالاعتماد المدافعة المحسوسة للجسم، لما يمنعه من الحركة إلى جهة فيكون من الكيفيات الملموسة ولا يقع اشتباه في تحققه ومغايرته للحركة وللطبيعة، لكونه محسوساً يوجد حيث لا حركة، كما في الحجر المسكن في الجو، والزق المفتوح المسكن تحت الماء، وينعدم مع بقاء الطبيعة كما في الجسم الساكن في حيزه الطبيعي، وقد يراد به مبدأ المدافعة فيفسر بكيفية يكون بها الجسم مدافعاً لما يمنعه عن الحركة إلى جهة ما، وسيأتي بيان تحققه ومغايرته للطبيعة ويبقى الاشتباه في أنه من أي قسم من أقسام الكيف..؟ قال : وقد يجعل أنواعه ستة بحسب الجهات، إلا أن الطبيعي منها إنما يكون إلى فوق أو تحت لما أنهما الجهتان الحقيقيتان، والبواقي إضافية تتبدل فلا تكون أنواعاً . على أن الحصر في الست عرفي لا حقيقي، إذ الجهات متكثرة جداً كأجزاء الجسم، أو غير منحصرة أصلاً كانقساماته . فالطبيعي من الاعتماد الثقل، وهي كيفية تقتضي حركة الجسم إلى حيث ينطبق مركزه على مركز العالم، وإلى صوب المركز في أكثر المسافة وبين المحيط من غير أن يبلغه والخفة وهي العكس . قال : وقد تجعل أنواعه أي أنواع الاعتماد ستة بحسب الحركات في الجهات الست ويدعى تضادها مطلقاً إن لم تشترط بين المتضادين غاية الخلاف، وإن اشترط انحصر التضاد فيما بين المتقابلين كالاعتماد والصاعد والهابط مثلاً، وفي جعل أنواع الاعتماد شيئاً ضعف من وجهين : أحدهما أن الاعتماد الطبيعي الذي يتصور فيه الاختلاف بالحقيقة إنما الصاعد والهابط أعني الميل إلى العلو والسفل اللذين هما الجهتان الحقيقتيان اللتان لا يتبدلان أصلاً، حتى لو تنكس الإنسان لم يصر فوقه تحتاً، وتحته فوقاً، بل صار رجله إلى فوق ورأسه إلى تحت، بخلاف سائر الجهات، فإنها إضافية تتبدل كالمواجه للمشرق إذا واجه المغرب صار قدامه خلفاً، شمالاً، وبالعكس، فتتبدل الاعتمادات أي يصير اعتماده إلى قدام اعتماداً إلى خلف وبالعكس ، وكذا إلى اليمين والشمال فلا يكون أنواعاً مختلفة . وثانيهما: أن حصر الجهات في الستة أمر عرفي، اعتبره العوام من الإنسان في أن له رأساً،وقدماً، وظهراً وبطناً، ويميناً وشمالاً، والخواص بخلاف ذلك.
He said: The second investigation: from the tangible things is i'timad (reliance/pressure), for those who make it the same as the sensed resistance (mudafa'a) which prevents motion to a direction, not its intelligible principle. He said: i'timad may be intended as the sensed resistance of the body to what prevents it from moving to a direction, thus it is among the tangible qualities and no doubt occurs about its reality and its being different from motion and from nature, because it is sensed, existing where there is no motion, as in a stone suspended in the air, and an open waterskin suspended under water, and it ceases while nature remains, as in a body resting in its natural place. And it may be intended as the principle of resistance, thus it is interpreted as a quality by which the body resists what prevents it from moving to some direction, and its reality and difference from nature will come later, and the doubt remains about which category of quality it belongs to. He said: And its types may be made six according to directions, except that the natural among them is only upward or downward because they are the real directions, and the rest are relative, changeable, so they are not types. However, the enumeration as six is conventional, not real, since directions are very numerous like the parts of the body, or not limited at all like its divisions. So the natural i'timad is heaviness (thiqal), which is a quality necessitating the motion of the body to where its center coincides with the center of the world, and towards the center over most of the distance and between the circumference without reaching it, and lightness (khiffa) is the opposite. He said: And its types, i.e. the types of i'timad, may be made six according to motions in the six directions, and their mutual opposition is claimed absolutely if no extreme opposition is required between contraries, and if required, opposition is limited to opposites like the ascending and descending i'timad, for example. And making types of i'timad something has weakness from two aspects: First, that natural i'timad in which difference is conceived in reality is only ascending and descending, i.e. the inclination to height and depth, which are the two real directions that never change, so that even if a person is inverted, his top does not become bottom nor his bottom top, but his foot becomes above and his head below, unlike other directions, for they are relative, changeable, like one facing east if he faces west, his front becomes back, north, and vice versa, so the i'timads change, i.e. his i'timad forward becomes i'timad backward and vice versa, and likewise to right and left, so they are not different types. Second, that the enumeration of directions as six is a conventional matter, considered by the common people based on man's having a head, feet, back, belly, right and left, while the specialists are otherwise.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage from al-Taftazani's Sharh al-Maqasid presents a detailed discussion of the concept of i'timad (often translated as 'pressure' or 'reliance'), a quality in the category of tangible things (al-malmūsāt). The author begins by defining i'timad from two perspectives. First, i'timad can refer to the actual sensed resistance that prevents a body from moving in a certain direction; in this sense it is a tangible quality, clearly distinct from motion and from the body's nature, because it can be sensed even when no motion occurs, as in the case of a stone suspended in air or a water-filled skin submerged but stationary. It can also cease while the body's nature remains, as in a body resting in its natural place. Second, i'timad can mean the principle or source of that resistance, interpreted as a quality by which the body resists what would move it. The doubt remains whether this second meaning is a type of quality (kayf) and which subdivision it belongs to—this will be clarified later. The author then moves to the classification of i'timad according to directions. He notes that some make six types corresponding to the six directions, but he criticizes this. The truly natural i'timad is only upward and downward, because these are the real, unchanging directions. Other directions (front, back, right, left) are relative and change depending on orientation, so i'timad associated with them is not a distinct type. Moreover, the enumeration of six directions is merely conventional, based on human anatomy, not real. The natural i'timad is further divided into heaviness (thiqal), which drives a body toward the center of the world, and lightness (khiffa), which drives it away from the center. The author then presents a second opinion that makes six types based on motions in the six directions and discusses the issue of opposition between them. Finally, he exposes two weaknesses in the six-type classification: first, only ascending and descending i'timad are truly different in kind; second, the sixfold division of directions is conventional, not real. Thus, the passage systematically defines i'timad, distinguishes its meanings, and refutes an overextended classification.

Key Terms
الاعتماد
الثقل
الخفة
الجهات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: I'timad has six distinct types corresponding to the six spatial directions (up, down, front, back, right, left).
Correction: Al-Taftazani argues that only upward and downward i'timad (heaviness and lightness) are real types; the others are not genuinely distinct because their directions are relative and changeable.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Taftazani reject the six-type division of i'timad?
A) Because he believes i'timad is not a quality at all.
B) Because only upward and downward i'timad are based on real directions, and the sixfold division is merely conventional.
C) Because the six types contradict Islamic teachings.
D) Because he thinks all i'timad is essentially the same.

Key Takeaway
Natural i'timad is only heaviness (downward) and lightness (upward); the sixfold classification is conventional and not based on real differences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Taftazani shows that the six directions are merely conventional based on human anatomy. In your own life, what 'directions'—beliefs, priorities, or identities—have you assumed are absolute simply because they are customary or widely accepted? How might you test whether they are as real as you think?
?
The example of inversion shows that up and down remain real no matter how you turn. In your spiritual journey, what is your 'upward'—the orientation toward Allah that never changes even when everything around you shifts? Are you currently acting as if that orientation depends on your situation?
?
The passage teaches that real differences are few; many categories we insist on are conventional. Is there an area where you have been multiplying distinctions (e.g., between people, between practices) that are not truly essential? What would it look like to focus only on the real, unchanging difference?

Five Discussions on Visible Things
Taftazani enumerates five discussions on light and color.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five key discussions that Taftazani enumerates regarding visible things, specifically light and color.

Core Idea
Taftazani distills the science of visible perceptions into five precise problems.

In this brief passage, Taftazani lays out the roadmap for the discussion of visible things (al-mubsirāt). He lists five distinct topics that will be treated in detail. This enumeration signals the key issues: the extremes of color, the reality of color, the nature of light, its occurrence, and its distinction from color.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the outline of the five discussions.
Notice the numbering and how each point is phrased.
2
Identify each discussion
List the five topics in order: color's ends, denial of color's reality, light as essential, occurrence of light, and light distinct from color.
3
Recognize the scope
Understand that these are headings for deeper analysis later in the text. The passage itself does not elaborate.
4
Reflect on key terms
Note the terms 'لون' (color), 'ضوء' (light), and 'طرفان' (two extremes). Consider what 'two ends' might mean.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني المبصرات وفيه مباحث الأول : للون طرفان الثاني : لا حقيقة للون في زعم بعض الناس الثالث : الضوء ذاتي الرابع : حدوث الضوء في المستضيء قد يكون من مضيء الخامس : الضوء مغاير للون
The second type: visible things. And it contains discussions: First: Color has two ends. Second: Color has no reality according to some people. Third: Light is essential. Fourth: The occurrence of light in the illuminated may be from an illuminator. Fifth: Light is different from color.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of five topics (mabāhith) that will be discussed in the section on visible perceptions (al-mubsirāt). The author, Taftazani, begins by stating 'The second type: visible things,' indicating the division of the discussion. He then lists five points: (1) Color has two ends (i.e., extremes or limits). (2) Some people claim that color has no reality. (3) Light is essential (dhātī) in its nature. (4) The occurrence of light in an illuminated object may originate from an illuminator. (5) Light is different from color. The author does not elaborate further in this passage; he merely provides the headings for the subsequent discussions.

Key Terms
المبصرات
اللون
الضوء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'طرفان' means 'two sides' rather than 'two extremes/endpoints' in color theory.
Correction: In this context, 'طرفان' refers to the extreme limits or endpoints of color, not physical sides.

Checkpoint
How many discussions are listed under the second type (visible things)?
A) Three
B) Four
C) Five
D) Six

Key Takeaway
Taftazani structures the study of visible things into five key discussions on color and light.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Taftazani lists a discussion that some people deny the reality of color. Have you ever accepted something as obvious (like color) without questioning its fundamental nature? Are you willing to examine one seemingly obvious belief today, even if it unsettles you?
?
The third discussion says light is essential. Do you take the light of knowledge and guidance in your life for granted, or do you recognize it as a gift from the One who is Light? What would change if you truly lived with that awareness starting now?
?
You have just read five headings that Taftazani deemed important enough to dedicate discussions to. What are the five most important categories you use to structure your own understanding of the world? Are they built on solid ground, or do they need re-examination?

Primary and Secondary Objects of Sight
Enumerates primary and secondary objects of sight

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' distinction between primary objects of sight (light and color) and secondary objects (shapes, motion, etc.) as explained by al-Sanusi.

Core Idea
Light and color are directly seen; everything else is seen through them.

In this passage, al-Sanusi presents the second type of perceptible objects: visible things. He explains that according to the philosophers, only light and color are seen directly, while other attributes like shapes, distances, and even human expressions are seen indirectly through these primary objects. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping the scope of visual perception in Islamic philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on primary objects
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning up to the list of secondary objects. Focus on the philosophers' claim that light and color are seen 'first and essentially'.
Notice that color is conditioned by light.
2
Identify the secondary objects
List all the secondary objects mentioned, such as straightness, curvature, length, motion, etc. Categorize them into shapes, spatial relations, and other attributes.
The list includes both physical and expressive attributes like laughter and beauty.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why the philosophers limit direct sight to light and color. How does this affect our understanding of perception?
This sets the stage for later discussions about indirect perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: النوع الثاني المبصرات كالألوان والأضواء، وقد يبصر بتوسطهما غيرهما، بل غير الكيفيات من الأوضاع والمقادير وما يتصل بها وها هنا بحث، ولها مباحث. ذهبت الفلاسفة إلى أن المبصر أولاً وبالذات هو الضوء واللون، وإن كان الثاني مشروطاً بالأول، وقد يبصر بتوسطهما ما لا يعد في الكيفيات المحسوسة من الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات من المقادير والأوضاع وغير ذلك، كالاستقامة والانحناء والتحدب والتقعر، وسائر الأشكال وكالطول والقصر والصغر والقرب، والبعد والتفرق، والاتصال والانفصال والحركة والسكون والضحك والبكاء والحسن والقبح، وغير ذلك. وأما ما يتوهم من إبصار مثل الرطوبة واليبوسة والملاسة والخشونة فمبني على أنه يبصر ملزوماتها كالسيلان والتماسك الراجعين إلى الحركة والسكون وكاستواء الأجزاء في الوضع واختلافها فيه.
He said: The second type: visible objects, such as colors and lights, and through these two, other things may be seen, indeed other than qualities, such as positions, magnitudes, and what pertains to them. And here there is a discussion, and it has topics. The philosophers held that the object seen primarily and essentially is light and color, although the second is conditioned by the first. And through these two may be seen what is not counted among the sensible qualities, from qualities specific to quantities, such as magnitudes, positions, and other things, like straightness, curvature, convexity, concavity, and all shapes, and like length, shortness, smallness, nearness, distance, separation, connection, disconnection, motion, rest, laughter, weeping, beauty, ugliness, and others. As for what is imagined of seeing such as wetness, dryness, smoothness, and roughness, it is based on the fact that one sees their necessary concomitants, like flow and coherence, which go back to motion and rest, and like the evenness of parts in position and their difference therein.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the second type of perceptible objects, namely visible objects (al-mubṣirāt). The author begins by stating that visible objects include colors and lights, and through these two, other things can be seen—even things that are not qualities, such as positions and magnitudes. He then presents the philosophers' position: the object seen primarily and essentially (awwalan wa bi al-dhāt) is light and color, though the second (color) is conditioned by the first (light). Through these primary objects, secondary objects are perceived, including various shapes (like straightness, curvature), dimensions (length, shortness), spatial relations (nearness, distance, separation, connection), motion and rest, human expressions (laughter, weeping), and aesthetic attributes (beauty, ugliness). Finally, the author addresses a potential misunderstanding: qualities like wetness, dryness, smoothness, and roughness are not directly seen; rather, what is seen are their necessary concomitants (malzūmāt), such as flow (sīlān, implying wetness) or coherence (tamāsuk, implying dryness), which reduce to motion/rest, and evenness or unevenness of parts in position. The passage thus clarifies the scope and limitations of visual perception, emphasizing that many perceived attributes are indirectly inferred.

Key Terms
المبصرات (visible objects)
الضوء (light)
اللون (color)
الكيفيات (qualities)
الكميات (quantities)
المقادير (magnitudes)
الأوضاع (positions)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers in the passage, what is the primary object of sight?
A) Shapes and magnitudes
B) Light and color
C) Motion and rest
D) Laughter and beauty

Key Takeaway
The philosophers held that light and color are the only objects seen directly; all other visible attributes are perceived through them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at a person's face, do you realize that you are only directly seeing light and color, and your mind constructs the shapes and expressions? How does this affect your trust in your own perception?
?
If light and color are the only direct objects of sight, then what does this say about the reliability of your visual experience? Are you comfortable knowing that much of what you 'see' is interpreted rather than directly sensed?
?
The source says even beauty and ugliness are seen through light and color. Think of a moment when you judged someone's appearance. Were you really directly perceiving beauty, or were you interpreting light and color? How might this realization change your judgments?

Seeing Wetness and Dryness: Concomitants vs. Direct Perception
Corrects a misconception about directly seeing wetness/dryness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that qualities like wetness and dryness are not directly seen, but rather their necessary concomitants, such as flow and coherence.

Core Idea
Wetness is not seen; only its effects on motion and position are.

Al-Sanusi addresses a potential misunderstanding: one might think we directly see qualities like wetness, dryness, smoothness, or roughness. He explains that what we actually see are their necessary concomitants—like the flow of liquid or the evenness of parts—which reduce to motion, rest, and positional relations. This clarification refines the earlier claim about secondary objects.

Guided Path
1
Read the correction passage
Read the final sentence of the main passage starting with 'وأما ما يتوهم'. Notice it begins by mentioning a common illusion ('يتوهم').
The phrase 'يتوهم' indicates that people mistakenly think they see these qualities directly.
2
Identify the alleged directly perceived qualities
List the qualities mentioned: wetness (الرطوبة), dryness (اليبوسة), smoothness (الملاسة), roughness (الخشونة).
These are often thought to be visible, but the author corrects this.
3
Understand the actual objects of sight
The passage states that what is seen are the 'necessary concomitants' (ملزومات) of these qualities, such as flow (السيلان) and coherence (التماسك), which reduce to motion/rest and evenness of parts.
Flow implies wetness; coherence implies dryness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: النوع الثاني المبصرات كالألوان والأضواء، وقد يبصر بتوسطهما غيرهما، بل غير الكيفيات من الأوضاع والمقادير وما يتصل بها وها هنا بحث، ولها مباحث. ذهبت الفلاسفة إلى أن المبصر أولاً وبالذات هو الضوء واللون، وإن كان الثاني مشروطاً بالأول، وقد يبصر بتوسطهما ما لا يعد في الكيفيات المحسوسة من الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات من المقادير والأوضاع وغير ذلك، كالاستقامة والانحناء والتحدب والتقعر، وسائر الأشكال وكالطول والقصر والصغر والقرب، والبعد والتفرق، والاتصال والانفصال والحركة والسكون والضحك والبكاء والحسن والقبح، وغير ذلك. وأما ما يتوهم من إبصار مثل الرطوبة واليبوسة والملاسة والخشونة فمبني على أنه يبصر ملزوماتها كالسيلان والتماسك الراجعين إلى الحركة والسكون وكاستواء الأجزاء في الوضع واختلافها فيه.
He said: The second type: visible objects, such as colors and lights, and through these two, other things may be seen, indeed other than qualities, such as positions, magnitudes, and what pertains to them. And here there is a discussion, and it has topics. The philosophers held that the object seen primarily and essentially is light and color, although the second is conditioned by the first. And through these two may be seen what is not counted among the sensible qualities, from qualities specific to quantities, such as magnitudes, positions, and other things, like straightness, curvature, convexity, concavity, and all shapes, and like length, shortness, smallness, nearness, distance, separation, connection, disconnection, motion, rest, laughter, weeping, beauty, ugliness, and others. As for what is imagined of seeing such as wetness, dryness, smoothness, and roughness, it is based on the fact that one sees their necessary concomitants, like flow and coherence, which go back to motion and rest, and like the evenness of parts in position and their difference therein.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the second type of perceptible objects, namely visible objects (al-mubṣirāt). The author begins by stating that visible objects include colors and lights, and through these two, other things can be seen—even things that are not qualities, such as positions and magnitudes. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that qualities like wetness, dryness, smoothness, and roughness are directly seen by the eye.
Correction: The passage clarifies that what is directly seen are their necessary concomitants, such as flow (for wetness) and coherence (for dryness), which reduce to motion/rest and evenness of parts.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is actually seen when we perceive wetness?
A) The wetness itself
B) The flow of liquid (sīlān)
C) The color of the liquid
D) The temperature of the liquid

Key Takeaway
Qualities like wetness and dryness are not directly perceived; they are inferred from visible concomitants such as flow and coherence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you were certain you saw something directly—like the wetness of rain. After reading this passage, can you honestly say you saw the wetness or just its effects? How does this humility about sensory knowledge apply to your certainty about unseen realities?
?
The author says it is 'imagined' (يتوهم) that we see wetness. What in your life might be based on 'imagining' that you perceive something directly when really you're only seeing its concomitants? How does this lesson call you to re-examine your assumptions about what you know 'for sure'?
?
If even basic sensory qualities like wetness are inferred, not directly seen, how much more careful must you be when claiming to have direct knowledge of spiritual realities? Will you start paying attention to the difference between what you actually see and what you infer?

Visible Things: Primary and Secondary Objects of Sight
Al-Taftazani enumerates the philosophers' view on visible things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' distinction between primary and secondary objects of sight, and how attributes like shapes and motions are perceived through light and color.

Core Idea
Sight directly perceives only light and color; everything else is inferred.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid introduces the second category of sensible things: visible objects. Al-Taftazani presents the philosophical view that while we seem to see many attributes, only light and color are directly perceived. All other visible qualities are seen through these two primary objects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage has a clear structure: definition, primary objects, secondary objects, and a misconception.
2
Identify the primary objects
The text states that what is seen 'first and essentially' (awwalan wa bi-l-dhāt) is light and color. Note that color is conditional upon light.
3
List the secondary objects
The passage gives many examples: shapes, sizes, distances, motion, rest, even laughter and beauty. These are seen through light and color, not directly.
These are all non-qualities, i.e., not sensible qualities themselves but properties of quantities.
4
Address the misconception
Consider the claim that wetness, dryness, smoothness, and roughness are seen. The philosophers say they are inferred from their necessary concomitants (luzūm), such as flowing (motion) and cohesion (rest).
This reinforces the principle that only light and color are directly perceived.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني المبصرات (قال): النوع الثاني المبصرات كالألوان والأضواء، وقد يبصر بتوسطهما غيرهما، بل غير الكيفيات من الأوضاع والمقادير وما يتصل بها وها هنا بحث، ولها مباحث). ذهبت الفلاسفة إلى أن المبصر أولاً وبالذات هو الضوء واللون، وإن كان الثاني مشروطاً بالأول، وقد يبصر بتوسطهما ما لا يعد في الكيفيات المحسوسة من الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات من المقادير والأوضاع وغير ذلك، كالاستقامة والانحناء والتحدب والتقعر، وسائر الأشكال وكالطول والقصر والصغر والقرب، والبعد والتفرق، والاتصال والانفصال والحركة والسكون والضحك والبكاء والحسن والقبح ، وغير ذلك. وأما ما يتوهم من إبصار مثل الرطوبة واليبوسة والملاسة والخشونة فمبني على أنه يبصر ملزوماتها كالسيلان والتماسك الراجعين إلى الحركة والسكون وكاستواء الأجزاء في الوضع واختلافها فيه .
The second type: visible things (al-mubsirat) – colors and lights, and through them other things are seen, indeed non-qualities such as positions, magnitudes, and what relates to them. And here there is a discussion, and it has topics. The philosophers held that the primarily and essentially (awwalan wa bi-l-dhāt) seen is light (al-ḍaw') and color (al-lawn), although the second is conditional upon the first; and through them may be seen what is not counted among sensible qualities from among the qualities specific to quantities such as magnitudes, positions, and other than that, like straightness, curvature, convexity, concavity, and all shapes; and like length, shortness, smallness, proximity, distance, separation, connection, disconnection, motion, rest, laughter, crying, beauty, ugliness, and other than that. As for what is imagined of seeing such as wetness, dryness, smoothness, roughness, it is based on the fact that one sees their implications (malzumāt), such as flowing and cohesion, which reduce to motion and rest, and like the uniformity of parts in position and their difference in it.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of sensible objects: visible things (al-mubsirat). It begins by stating that visible things include colors and lights, and through these two, other things are perceived—specifically, non-qualities like positions and magnitudes. The author then presents the philosophical position: that the primary and direct object of sight is light and color, with color being conditional upon light. Through these, attributes not counted as sensible qualities are perceived, such as shapes (straight, curved, convex, concave), dimensions (long, short, small), spatial relations (near, far), continuity/discontinuity, motion/rest, and even human states like laughter, crying, beauty, and ugliness. Finally, the author addresses a misconception: the supposed sight of wetness, dryness, smoothness, and roughness. These are not directly seen but are inferred from their necessary concomitants (luzūm): flowing (which reduces to motion), cohesion (rest), and uniformity or irregularity in part positions. Thus, the passage clarifies the philosophers' rigorous distinction between primary and secondary visible objects.

أولاً وبالذات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: We directly see wetness, dryness, smoothness, and roughness.
Correction: These are not directly seen but inferred from the observation of their necessary concomitants (luzūm), such as flowing (motion) and cohesion (rest), or uniformity/irregularity of parts.

Checkpoint

B) Light and color

D) Wetness and dryness

Key Takeaway
The philosophers hold that sight directly perceives only light and color; all other visible attributes are perceived through these two.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone laughing or crying, do you typically think you directly perceive their emotion, or do you realize you only see colors and shapes that you then interpret? How often does your mind mistakenly treat inferences as direct observations, and what does that mean for your certainty about what you 'see'?
?
If sight only ever directly perceives light and color, then almost everything you 'see' throughout your day—people, objects, expressions—is actually a construction of your mind. How does that change your confidence in your senses as a source of truth in matters of faith?
?
The philosophers argue that many apparent qualities (like beauty, ugliness, wetness) are only seen through their implications. In your own life, are you prone to mistaking your immediate impressions for the reality of a situation? Can you recall a time when something you 'saw' turned out to be an inference, not a direct perception?

Argument from Reflection of Colors and the Objection on Mixing
The author presents the second proof from reflection and engages with objections about color composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the reflection of colors challenges the theory that colors are composed from black and white, and how dialectical responses refine the argument without disproving it.

Core Idea
Reflection of colors disproves necessary composition from black and white.

This passage presents a sophisticated argument about the nature of colors using the phenomenon of reflection. The author shows that if colors were necessarily composed of black and white, then only white would reflect—contradicting experience. He then anticipates and responds to objections, demonstrating the kalām method of dialectical reasoning. Working through this passage will sharpen your ability to follow multi-step logical arguments and appreciate the nuance of theological-philosophical debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for overall flow
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then the translation. Note that it contains a main proof followed by several objections and responses.
The passage is dense; read slowly and track each objection and reply.
2
Identify the main proof from reflection
The author states: if color difference were due to mixing of transparent body with something else, then only white would reflect from red and green, because black does not reflect by experience. This is the second proof.
The key premise is that black does not reflect; thus red and green (mixtures) should not reflect black, but they do reflect red and green, contradicting the composition theory.
3
Follow the objections and responses
The passage then presents objections: (1) composition can occur in various ways, (2) whiteness peels off and locus accepts colors unlike blackness. The author responds by distinguishing real vs. imaginary and clarifying the nature of acceptance.
Each objection is countered with a precise logical distinction. Note how the author uses conditional reasoning.
4
Summarize the conclusion
The overall conclusion is that the reflection argument shows the cause of color differences is not necessarily composition from black and white; it is more evident against that theory than against the alternative (air mixing). The objections do not refute the argument but refine it.
The passage ends with a final refutation of a claim about receptivity and actuality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانيهما: انعكاس الحمرة والخضرة، ونحو ذلك من الألوان فإنه لو كان اختلاف الألوان لاختلاط الشفاف بغيره لوجب أن لا ينعكس من الأحمر والأخضر إلا البياض، لأن السواد لا ينعكس بحكم التجربة. ودلالة هذين الوجهين على أن سبب اختلاف الألوان لا يجب أن يكون هو التركب من السواد والبياض، أظهر من دلالته على أن سبب البياض لا البياض لا يجب أن يكون مخالطة الهواء للأجزاء الشفافة أن في الملازمتين نظراً، لجواز أن يقع مع تركب السواد والبياض على أنحاء مختلفة، وأن ينعكس السواد عند الاختلاط والامتزاج، وإن لم ينعكس عند الانفراد، وقد اقتصر بعضهم على نفي البياض، وأثبت السواد تمسكاً بأن البياض ينسلخ ويقبل محله الألوان بخلاف السواد، ورد بعد ثبوت الأمرين بأنه : يجوز أن يكون الحقيقي مفارقاً والتخيلي لازماً، لزوال سبب الأول، ولزوم سبب التخيلي، لا يقال محل(١) البياض يقبل محله الألوان وكل ما يقبل الشيء فهو عار عنه، ضرورة تنافي القبول والفعل لأنا نجيب بمنع الصغرى فإنه إنما يقبل ما سوى البياض الذي فيه فلا يلزم إلا عراؤه عنه ، وإن أريد بالقبول معنى الإمكان بحيث يجامع الفعل منعنا الكبرى وهو ظاهر. جميع لكان الفعل (۲) ممتنع وقد يقال : لو كان القابل للشيء واجب العراء عنه لكان الاتصاف به وهو باطل وليس بشيء لأن القضية مشروطة، فلا يلزم إلا امتناع الاتصاف ما دام قابلا وهو حق .
Second: The reflection of redness and greenness, and other such colors. For if the difference of colors were due to the mixing of the transparent body with something else, then it would be necessary that nothing but whiteness reflect from red and green, because black does not reflect by the rule of experience. The indication of these two points—that the cause of the difference of colors must not be composition from black and white—is more evident than its indication that the cause of whiteness must not be the mixture of air with transparent particles, because in the two implications there is consideration, since it is possible that composition from black and white occurs in various ways, and that black reflects at the time of mixture and blending, though it does not reflect when isolated. And some confined themselves to denying whiteness, and affirmed blackness, holding fast to the fact that whiteness peels off and its locus accepts colors, unlike blackness. And it was refuted after establishing both matters by [saying]: It is possible that the real one is separable and the imaginary is concomitant, due to the removal of the cause of the first and the persistence of the cause of the imaginary. It is not said: The locus of whiteness accepts colors other than itself, and everything that accepts a thing is devoid of it, due to the necessity of the incompatibility of acceptance and actuality—for we reply by denying the minor premise: it only accepts what is other than the whiteness in it, so nothing follows except its being devoid of it. And if by acceptance is meant the meaning of possibility such that it coexists with actuality, we deny the major premise, and that is apparent. All [?] would be impossible. And it may be said: If that which is receptive to a thing were necessarily devoid of it, then its being qualified by it would be false—and it is nothing, because the proposition is conditional, so nothing follows except the impossibility of being qualified as long as it is receptive, and that is true.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed argument about the nature of color composition, specifically the claim that the difference of colors is due to mixing of black and white. The author presents the second point of proof from the phenomenon of reflection: if colors were mixtures of black and white, then only white would reflect from red and green, since black does not reflect. He then notes that the indication of this proof is more evident against the necessity of composition from black and white than against the alternative theory that whiteness comes from mixing air with transparent parts. He then addresses objections: some have limited the denial to whiteness only and affirmed blackness, arguing that whiteness can be removed and its locus accepts other colors unlike blackness. The response is that the real (essential) whiteness might be separable while the imaginary (apparent) is concomitant. Then a further objection: a locus that accepts whiteness is devoid of it—the answer is that it only accepts what is other than the whiteness currently present, so it is only devoid of that whiteness, not of whiteness in general. If acceptance means potential, it can coexist with actuality. Finally, the author refutes a claim that if a receptacle necessarily lacked the thing it receives, then it could never be qualified by it—this is false because the proposition is conditional, only implying impossibility while it is receptive. This is a classic dialectical argument in kalam concerning physical qualities.

Key Terms
انعكاس
السواد
البياض
التركيب
اختلاط
قبول
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof from reflection conclusively disproves the composition theory.
Correction: The proof only shows that composition from black and white is not the necessary cause; alternative explanations remain possible, as the objections show.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the argument from reflection in this passage?
A) That the difference of colors must be due to composition from black and white.
B) That the cause of color differences is necessarily the mixture of air with transparent parts.
C) That the cause of color differences is not necessarily composition from black and white.
D) That white reflects because it is a mixture of air and transparent bodies.

Key Takeaway
The phenomenon of color reflection shows that the cause of color differences is not necessarily the composition of black and white.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face objections to your faith, do you engage with them as carefully as the author engages with these objections, or do you dismiss them because you fear the answer? What specific doubt have you been avoiding, and will you seek to understand it rather than suppress it?
?
The author distinguishes between real whiteness and imaginary whiteness. In your own spiritual life, are you satisfied with an imaginary faith—one that appears pious but lacks reality? What would it mean for you to seek the real, essential connection with Allah, even if it requires stripping away pretense?
?
This passage teaches that what seems obvious (that color reflection disproves composition) may need refinement. Where in your understanding of Islam have you settled for a simplistic explanation because it feels comfortable? Will you commit to digging deeper, even if it means acknowledging complexity?

The debate over primary colors and their composition
The author presents two opinions on primary colors and their mixtures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical debate in Islamic theology about which colors are primary and how observational evidence was used to support different views.

Core Idea
The debate over primary colors reveals how observation alone cannot yield universal theological certainty.

In this passage, the author presents two competing theories about the primary colors in the context of kalam natural philosophy. One theory holds that only white and black are primary, while another adds red, yellow, and green. The author notes that both rely on observation in particular cases, which falls short of universal judgment. As you read, notice the detailed mixtures described in the first theory and see how the second theory expands the list of primaries.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two opinions
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Mark where the first opinion (white and black only) is stated, and where the second opinion (adding red, yellow, green) appears.
The author introduces both opinions early: 'قيل : الأصل هو البياض والسواد' and 'وقيل : والحمرة والصفرة والخضرة أيضاً'.
2
Examine the detailed mixture examples
Focus on the first opinion's color combinations: white+black yields dust-color; black+light with dominance yields red, dark gray, yellow; then further mixtures produce green, leek-color, indigo, purple. Try to follow the logic of each step.
This systematic progression is meant to show that all colors can be derived from white and black alone.
3
Consider the author's critical note
Read the closing remarks: 'ولا يخفى أنها لا تفيد الحكم الكلي' (it does not yield universal judgment). Reflect on why observational evidence in particular cases might be insufficient for establishing universal theological truths.
This critique applies to both opinions, reminding us that kalam demands certainty beyond empirical observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السواد والبياض أصل الألوان قال: وقيل : الأصل هو البياض والسواد ، وقيل : والحمرة والصفرة والخضرة أيضاً، والبواقي بالتركيب تعويلاً (۳) على مشاهدة ذلك في بعض الصور، ولا يخفى أنها لا تفيد الحكم الكلي). القائلون بكون السواد والبياض كيفيتين حقيقيتين منهم من زعم أنهما أصل الألوان والبواقي بالتركيب لما نشاهد من أن البياض والسواد إن اختلطا وحدهما حصلت الغبرة، وإن خالط السواد ضوءاً كما في الغمامة التي تشرق عليها الشمس والدخان الذي يخالطه النار، فإن كان السواد غالباً حصلت الحمرة، وإن اشتدت الغلبة حصلت القتمة وإن غلب الضوء حصلت الصفرة ثم إن الصفرة إذا خالطها سواد مشرق حصلت الخضرة ثم إن الخضرة إذا انضم إليها سواد آخر حصلت الكراثية (۱) ، وإذا انضم إليها بياض حصلت الزنجارية ، ثم الكراثية إن خلطها سواد وقليل حمرة حصلت النيلية، ثم النيلية إن خالطها حمرة حصلت الأرجوانية وعلى هذا القياس. ومنهم من زعم أن الأصل هو السواد والبياض والحمرة والصفرة الخضرة والبواقي بالتركيب بحكم المشاهدة ولا يخفى أنهما يفيدان التركيب المخصوص بقيد (۲) اللون المخصوص، وإما أن ذلك اللون لا يحصل إلا من هذا التركيب ولا يكون حقيقة مفردة فلا.
Black and white are the origin of colors. He said: And it was said: The origin is white and black. And it was said: [also] red, yellow, and green, and the rest are by composition, relying on observation in some cases, and it is not hidden that [observation] does not yield universal judgment. Those who hold that black and white are two real qualities—some of them claim that they are the origin of colors and the rest are by composition, because we observe that when white and black mix alone, dust-color results; and when light mixes with black—as in the cloud upon which the sun shines, and the smoke mixed with fire—if black is dominant, redness results; if dominance intensifies, dark gray results; if light dominates, yellowness results; then when yellowness mixes with bright black, greenness results; then when greenness is joined by another blackness, leek-color results; when joined by whiteness, verdigris-color results; then when leek-color is mixed with black and a little redness, indigo-color results; then when indigo-color is mixed with redness, purple-color results; and so on. And among them there are those who claim that the origin is black, white, red, yellow, and green, and the rest are by composition by virtue of observation; and it is not hidden that they [these theories] indicate a specific composition with a specific color, and that that color does not result except from this composition and is not a simple reality? No.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage discusses the theories of primary colors in the context of kalam physics. It first presents the view that white and black are the primary colors from which all others are composed by mixture. Then it presents an alternative view that red, yellow, and green are also primary. The author notes that these theories rely on observation in particular cases and do not yield universal certainty. The passage then elaborates on the first view (white and black as primaries) by giving a detailed account of how specific mixtures produce specific colors: dust-color from white and black; red from black dominant with light; dark gray from stronger dominance; yellow from light dominance; green from yellow with bright black; and further mixtures producing leek, verdigris, indigo, and purple. Then it mentions the second view that there are five primaries: white, black, red, yellow, green. The author comments that both views indicate that a specific composition yields a specific color, and implies that those colors might not be independent simple entities but only results of composition. This is a technical discussion showing the diversity of opinions in classical natural philosophy within Islamic theology.

Key Terms
السواد
البياض
الأصل
التركيب
الحمرة
الصفرة
الخضرة
المشاهدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the author endorses one view over the other.
Correction: The author merely presents both opinions and notes the limitation of observational evidence for both, without favoring either.

Checkpoint
According to the first opinion mentioned in this passage, which colors are considered original?
A) White, black, red, yellow, and green.
B) White and black only.
C) All seven colors of the rainbow.
D) Red, yellow, and green only.

Key Takeaway
The debate over primary colors illustrates that empirical observation alone cannot establish universal theological truths in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often trust your eyes to tell you the truth about the world. But this lesson shows that observation can be limited—it cannot give you universal certainty. Where in your life have you assumed that what you see is the whole truth? Will you now question your sensory conclusions before building beliefs on them?
?
The author does not pick a side in the debate. Are you comfortable with uncertainty, or do you feel the need to settle every question? Can you live with intellectual humility, acknowledging that some truths remain beyond your reach?
?
If empirical evidence cannot establish universal judgments, how do you justify the most important beliefs in your life—about God, the afterlife, or right and wrong? Have you relied on sensory experience for these, or do you have a deeper foundation? Resolve today to ground your core convictions on revelation and reason, not just observation.

Two Theories on the Composition of Colors
Discusses two opinions on which colors are original and which are composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two classical opinions on which colors are original and which are composite, including the logical sequences of color mixtures.

Core Idea
Colors are not all simple; many arise from specific mixtures of a few original colors.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid presents two competing theories about the origin of colors. The first theory claims that only black and white are original, and all other colors are produced by mixing these two with light or each other in specific proportions. The second theory expands the set of original colors to include red, yellow, and green. By studying these theories, we gain insight into how medieval scholars analyzed sensory phenomena through rational reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English to get an overview of the two opinions. Notice the structure and flow of the argument.
2
Identify the two opinions
Find where the first opinion ends and the second begins. Note the different sets of original colors in each opinion.
The first opinion starts with 'القائلون...' and the second with 'ومنهم من زعم...'.
3
Trace the mixture sequences
For the first opinion, follow the steps from black and white mixing to produce gray, red, yellow, etc. For the second, note the expanded list of originals.
The sequence is detailed: black+white = gray; black+light (if black dominates) = red, etc.
4
Understand the concluding remark
The passage ends with a note that these mixtures produce specific colors under specific conditions, and those composite colors are not simple realities.
This highlights the composite nature of many colors.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why scholars debated this — it relates to whether qualities are simple or composite, a key issue in physics and metaphysics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القائلون بكون السواد والبياض كيفيتين حقيقيتين منهم من زعم أنهما أصل الألوان والبواقي بالتركيب لما نشاهد من أن البياض والسواد إن اختلطا وحدهما حصلت الغبرة، وإن خالط السواد ضوءاً كما في الغمامة التي تشرق عليها الشمس والدخان الذي يخالطه النار، فإن كان السواد غالباً حصلت الحمرة، وإن اشتدت الغلبة حصلت القتمة وإن غلب الضوء حصلت الصفرة ثم إن الصفرة إذا خالطها سواد مشرق حصلت الخضرة ثم إن الخضرة إذا انضم إليها سواد آخر حصلت الكراثية، وإذا انضم إليها بياض حصلت الزنجارية، ثم الكراثية إن خلطها سواد وقليل حمرة حصلت النيلية، ثم النيلية إن خالطها حمرة حصلت الأرجوانية وعلى هذا القياس. ومنهم من زعم أن الأصل هو السواد والبياض والحمرة والصفرة الخضرة والبواقي بالتركيب بحكم المشاهدة ولا يخفى أنهما يفيدان التركيب المخصوص بقيد اللون المخصوص، وإما أن ذلك اللون لا يحصل إلا من هذا التركيب ولا يكون حقيقة مفردة فلا.
Those who say that black and white are two real qualities, among them are those who claimed that they are the origin of colors and the rest are by composition, based on what we observe: that if white and black mix alone, gray results; and if black mixes with light, as in a cloud on which the sun shines and smoke mixed with fire, then if black predominates, red results; if the predominance intensifies, dust-color (qatamah) results; if light predominates, yellow results; then if yellow mixes with bright black, green results; then if green is combined with another black, leek-green (kurrāthiyyah) results; if white is added, verdigris (zanjāriyyah) results; then if leek-green is mixed with black and a little red, indigo (nīliyyah) results; then if indigo is mixed with red, purple (urjuwāniyyah) results, and so on. And among them are those who claimed that the originals are black, white, red, yellow, and green, and the rest are by composition, by observation. It is not hidden that they indicate a specific composition under the condition of a specific color, and that that color is not obtained except from this composition and is not a simple reality.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage presents two opinions on the composition of colors. The first opinion holds that black and white are the only original colors, and all others result from various mixtures of these two with light or with each other. The author provides a detailed sequence of mixtures, showing how gray, red, yellow, green, leek-green, verdigris, indigo, and purple are produced. The second opinion expands the set of original colors to include red, yellow, and green along with black and white, and claims that the remaining colors are composite. The passage ends by asserting that these mixtures produce specific colors under specific conditions, and that such resulting colors are not simple entities but composite realities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think all colors are simple qualities that cannot be broken down further.
Correction: The passage shows that many colors like gray, red, green, etc., are produced by mixing original colors (black and white, or more), so they are composite, not simple.

Checkpoint
According to the first opinion in the passage, which colors are considered original?
A) Black and white only
B) Black, white, red, yellow, and green
C) All colors are original
D) Only primary colors of light

Key Takeaway
The composition of colors was debated among scholars; some held that only black and white are original, others added more originals, but all agreed that many colors are composite, not simple.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage shows that what appears simple (like red) is actually composite when traced back. What is one belief you hold that you have never questioned, assuming it was simple, but which might actually be composed of smaller, more fundamental truths? How would you begin to examine it?
?
If even the colors you see every day require precise conditions to produce, what does that suggest about the precision of Allah's creation? Take a moment to reflect on one natural color you observed today—does knowing its composition make you more aware of the order in creation? How will that change your next moment of gratitude?

The Two Types of Light: Essential and Accidental
Taftazani defines essential and accidental light with Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between essential light (ḍiyā') and accidental light (nūr) according to al-Taftazani, and their connection to the sun and moon as mentioned in the Quran.

Core Idea
Light is either essential (from itself) or accidental (derived from another).

In this passage, al-Taftazani establishes the foundational classification of light. He explains that the sun emits light from its own essence (called ḍiyā'), while the moon reflects light (called nūr). This distinction is grounded both in rational analysis and in the Quranic verse 'He who made the sun a ḍiyā' and the moon a nūr.'

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the definitions of essential and accidental light.
Notice the keywords: ḍiyā' and nūr.
2
Identify the two types
The passage presents two main categories: essential light (from the essence of the locus) and accidental light (supervening from another source). Note the examples: sun and moon.
3
Connect to Quranic proof
Taftazani cites the Quranic verse to support the distinction. Reflect on how the verse uses different terms for sun and moon.
The verse is a direct proof for the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث: الضوء ذاتي إن كان من ذات المحل كما للشمس ويسمى ضياء، وإلا فعرضي كما للقمر يسمى نوراً، وهو إن حصل من الضوء لذاته فأول كضوء ما يقابل الشمس، وإلا فثان وثالث وهلم جراً كضوء وجه الأرض قبل الطلوع، وضوء داخل البيت من الدار وهكذا إلى أن ينعدم وهو الظلمة فهي عدم ملكة له لا كيفية وجودية، وإلا لكان مانعاً للجالس في الغار من إبصار الخارج، كالعكس للقطع بعدم الفرق في الحائل ما يحيط بالرائي أو بالمرئي وليست عدماً صرفاً تنافياً للمجعولية من قوله تعالى: وجعل الظلمات والنور. غني عن التعريف كسائر المحسوسات وتعريفه بأنه كيفية هي كمال أول للشفاف من حيث هو شفاف، أو بأنه كيفية لا يتوقف الإبصار بها على الإبصار بشيء آخر تعريف بالأخفى، وكان المراد التنبيه على بعض الخواص، والضوء إن كان من ذات المحل بأن لا يكون فائضاً عليه من مقابلة جسم آخر مضيء فذاتي كما للشمس ويسمى ضياء وإلا فعرضي كما للقمر نوراً أخذاً من قوله تعالى: وهو الذي جعل الشمس ضياء أي ذات ضياء، والقمر نوراً أي ذا نور. ويسمى العوضي إن كان حصوله من مقابلة المضيء لذاته كضوء جرم القمر وضوء وجه الأرض المقابل للشمس فهو الضوء الأول، وإن كان من مقابلة المضيء لغيره كضوء وجه الأرض قبل طلوع الشمس من مقابلة الهواء المقابل للشمس، وكضوء داخل البيت الذي في الدار من مقابلة هواء الدار المضيء من مقابلة الهواء المقابل للشمس، أو لهواء آخر يقابلها، فهو الضوء الثاني والثالث وهلم جرا على اختلاف الوسائط بينه وبين المضيء بالذات، إلى أن ينتهي إلى الظلمة، أعني عدم الضوء عما كان له الضوء بالكلية وينعدم، وهو عدم ملكة للضوء لا كيفية وجودية على ما ذهب إليه البعض، وإلا لكان مانعاً للجالس في الغار من إبصار من هو في هواء مضيء خارج الغار، كما أنه مانع له من إبصار من هو في الغار، وذلك للقطع بعدم الفرق في ذلك.
The third section: Light: essential if it is from the essence of the locus as for the sun, and it is called diya' (radiance); otherwise accidental, as for the moon, called nur (light). And if it is obtained from light [coming] from the self, then it is first, like the light of what faces the sun; otherwise second, third, and so on, like the light of the earth's surface before sunrise, and the light inside the house from the courtyard, and so on until it ceases, and it is darkness, which is a privation of possession of it, not an existential quality. Otherwise it would prevent the one sitting in the cave from seeing the outside, and likewise the opposite, because of the certainty that there is no difference in the barrier whether it surrounds the seer or the seen, and it is not pure non-existence conflicting with being made [by God] from His statement: 'And He made darkness and light.' It is needless of definition like other sensed things, and defining it as a quality which is the first perfection of the transparent insofar as it is transparent, or as a quality such that vision by it does not depend on vision by something else (a definition by what is more obscure), and the intention was to call attention to some properties. And light: if it is from the essence of the locus in that it is not supervening upon it from facing another luminous body, then essential as for the sun and called diya'; otherwise accidental as for the moon, called nur, taken from His statement: 'It is He who made the sun a diya' i.e., possessing diya', and the moon a nur i.e., possessing nur. And it is called 'iwadi (substitutional) if its occurrence is from facing the luminous in itself, like the light of the moon's disk and the light of the earth's surface facing the sun; that is the first light. And if it is from facing the luminous for something else, like the light of the earth's surface before sunrise from facing the air facing the sun, and like the light inside the house in the courtyard from facing the air of the courtyard illuminated from facing the air facing the sun, or another air that faces it, then it is the second and third light, and so on, according to the difference in intermediaries between it and the essentially luminous, until it ends in darkness, meaning the absence of light from that which had light entirely, and it ceases. And it is a privation of possession of light, not an existential quality, according to what some hold. Otherwise it would prevent the one sitting in the cave from seeing someone in illuminated air outside the cave, just as it prevents him from seeing someone in the cave, because of the certainty that there is no difference in that.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of light (al-ḍaw') by al-Taftazani. He begins by stating the two main types: essential (dhātī) light, which originates from the essence of the locus (like the sun's light, called ḍiyā'), and accidental ('araḍī) light, which is derived from another source (like the moon's light, called nūr). He then further subdivides accidental light into degrees: the first light is directly from the luminous source (e.g., the moon itself or the earth's surface directly facing the sun); the second, third, etc. arise through intermediaries (e.g., the earth's surface before sunrise illuminated by air already lit by the sun). The series continues until light completely ceases, which is darkness (ẓulma). Darkness is defined as the privation ('adam milkah) of light, not an existential quality (kayfiyya wujūdiyya). Al-Taftazani argues that if darkness were a positive quality, it would block vision for someone in a cave seeing both inside and outside equally, which is false. He also notes that light is known through sensation, so definitions of light are merely indicative. The passage concludes by reaffirming the division: essential light (ḍiyā') versus accidental light (nūr), with a reference to the Quranic verse 'He who made the sun a ḍiyā' and the moon a nūr.' The term 'iwāḍī (substitutional) is introduced for accidental light that comes from facing the luminous source itself versus from facing something else. Throughout, the author maintains that darkness is not a positive entity but the mere absence of light, a key point in kalam physics.

Key Terms
ضوء
ضياء
نور
ذاتي
عرضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The terms ḍiyā' and nūr are interchangeable synonyms for light from any source.
Correction: Taftazani shows that ḍiyā' specifically denotes essential light (from the self), while nūr denotes accidental light (derived from another), as supported by the Quran.

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani, which type of light does the sun emit?
A) Accidental light (nūr)
B) Essential light (ḍiyā')
C) Substitutional light ('iwāḍī)
D) Reflected light

Key Takeaway
Essential light (ḍiyā') comes from the luminous body itself, while accidental light (nūr) is borrowed from another source, as exemplified by the sun and moon respectively.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own faith and character, do you find yourself radiating guidance from within (ḍiyā') or merely reflecting what you absorb from scholars and peers (nūr)? Which kind of light would you need to become for your light to endure even when external sources are absent?
?
The sun gives light without receiving from any other, while the moon borrows light and then disappears at dawn. In your daily spiritual life, are you a sun or a moon? What concrete step can you take today to develop essential light in your relationship with Allah?

Gradations of Light and the Nature of Darkness
Taftazani explains the gradation of accidental light and the privation nature of darkness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of substitutional light ('iwāḍī), the gradations of accidental light through intermediaries, and the argument that darkness is the privation of light, not a positive quality.

Core Idea
Darkness is not a thing but the absence of light.

After classifying light into essential and accidental, al-Taftazani explains how accidental light comes in degrees: from direct facing of the luminous source (first light) through multiple intermediaries (second, third, etc.) until it fades into darkness. He then refutes the view that darkness is a positive quality by using a thought experiment about a cave.

Guided Path
1
Trace the descending grades of light
Read how light weakens through intermediaries: first, second, third, and so on. Notice the examples: the moon's surface, the earth before sunrise, and inside a house.
2
Understand substitutional light ('iwāḍī)
The passage defines 'iwāḍī light as the first degree, obtained directly from the essentially luminous source. This is contrasted with later degrees.
3
Grasp the definition of darkness
Darkness is defined as the complete absence of light (a privation), not an existential quality. Taftazani emphasizes this with the cave argument.
4
Reflect on the cave argument
If darkness were a positive quality, it would block vision from inside the cave looking out just as it blocks looking in. This is false, so darkness is simply absence.

?

?

Definition and Classification of Light
Al-Taftazani defines light and classifies it into essential, accidental, and reflected types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani defines light and classifies it into essential, accidental, and reflected types with multiple degrees.

Core Idea
Light varies: from self-luminous sources to borrowed reflections.

This passage from al-Taftazani establishes a precise vocabulary for light, distinguishing between light that originates from a source itself and light that is borrowed from another. He even catalogues multiple degrees of reflected light, showing the sophistication of kalam physics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First read the Arabic passage and its English translation without analysing. Let the definitions and examples sink in.
Notice the Quranic citation supporting the terminology.
2
Identify the main categories
Find the three types: essential (dhātī), accidental (‘araḍī), and substitute (al-‘awaḍī). Observe the basis for each: origin in the substance or from another.
Substitute light is a subset of accidental light?
3
Trace the degrees of reflected light
Follow the examples: first light (direct from essentially luminous), second light (through one medium), third light (through two media), etc. Notice how the chain leads to darkness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للشفاف من حيث هو شفاف، أو بأنه كيفية لا يتوقف الإبصار بها على الإبصار بشيء آخر (تعريف بالأخفى، وكان المراد التنبيه على بعض الخواص، والضوء إن كان من ذات المحل بأن لا يكون فائضاً عليه من مقابلة مقابلة جسم آخر (۱) مضيء فذاتي كما للشمس ويسمى ضياء وإلا فعرضي كمـا للقمـر نوراً أخذاً من قوله تعالى : وهو الذي جعل الشمس ضياء أي ذات ضياء، والقمر نوراً) (٢) أي ذا نور. ويسمى العوضي إن كان حصوله من مقابلة المضيء لذاته (۳) كضوء جرم القمر وضوء وجه الأرض المقابل للمشس فهو الضوء الأول ، وإن كان من مقابلة النضيء لغيره كضوء وجه الأرض قبل طلوع الشمس من مقابلة الهواء المقابل للشمس، وكضوء داخل البيت الذي في الدار من مقابلة هواء الدار المضيء من مقابلة الهواء المقابل للشمس، أو لهواء آخر يقابلها، فهو الضوء الثاني والثالث وهلم جرا . على اختلاف الوسائط بينه وبين المضيء بالذات، إلى أن ينتهي إلى الظلمة
Concerning the transparent (shaffāf) insofar as it is transparent, or that it is a quality (kayfiyya) such that vision through it does not depend on seeing something else (a definition by the more obscure, and the intention was to draw attention to some properties. And light (ḍaw’), if it originates from the substance of the locus such that it does not overflow onto it from facing a facing of another luminous body, then it is essential (dhātī) as for the sun and is called brilliance (ḍiyā’); otherwise it is accidental (‘araḍī) as for the moon, called light (nūr), taking from His saying: 'And He made the sun a brilliance (ḍiyā’) i.e., possessing brilliance, and the moon a light (nūr)' i.e., possessing light. And it is called substitute (al-‘awaḍī) [perhaps compensatory] if its occurrence is by facing the essentially luminous, like the light of the moon's body and the light of the earth's surface facing the sun, so it is the first light. And if it is by facing that which is luminous by another, like the light of the earth's surface before sunrise from facing the air facing the sun, and like the light inside a house in a courtyard from facing the air of the courtyard which is illuminated from facing the air facing the sun, or another air facing it, so it is the second light, third, and so on, according to the difference of the media between it and the essentially luminous, until it ends at darkness.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage provides a definition and classification of light. The author first defines the transparent (shaffāf) as a quality that does not require seeing something else for vision through it. Then he classifies light into two main types: essential (dhātī) and accidental (‘araḍī). Essential light originates from the substance itself, like the sun, called brilliance (ḍiyā’). Accidental light is acquired from another source, like the moon, called light (nūr), supported by a Quranic verse (Yūnus 10:5). He then introduces a third type, substitute (al-‘awaḍī), which is reflected light, further divided into degrees: first light is direct reflection from the essentially luminous; second, third, etc., are indirect reflections through multiple media. The passage concludes by noting that light gradually diminishes until it reaches darkness.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Light is a simple, uniform phenomenon without degrees or types.
Correction: Al-Taftazani shows that light has essential (self-luminous) and accidental (borrowed) types, and accidental light can be further divided into multiple degrees of reflection.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes essential light from accidental light?
A) Essential light is from a source like the sun, while accidental light is from a source like the moon.
B) Essential light is reflected, while accidental light is direct.
C) Essential light is called nūr, while accidental light is called ḍiyā’.
D) Essential light requires a medium, while accidental light does not.

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani classifies light into essential (self-luminous) and accidental (borrowed), with reflected light having multiple degrees.

Darkness: Privation Not Positive Quality
Al-Taftazani argues darkness is privation, refutes opposing view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological argument that darkness is a privation of light and how the Quranic verse is reconciled with this view.

Core Idea
Darkness is not a thing but an absence—a specific non-existence made by God.

Al-Taftazani presents a nuanced discussion on the nature of darkness, arguing it is not a positive quality but merely the absence of light. He uses a cave analogy to refute the opposing view and addresses the Quranic verse 'He made the darknesses and the light.' This lesson unpacks the argument and the critical distinction between pure non-existence and specific non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the main thesis
Read the passage and identify Al-Taftazani's central claim: darkness is the absence of light, not a positive quality. Note the terms 'abd al-ḍaw' (absence of light) and 'malaka' (disposition).
2
Analyze the cave analogy
The passage presents a proof: if darkness were a positive quality, it would act as a barrier in the cave. Work through the logic and the objection raised.
The objection: lack of light is not a barrier but a missing condition.
3
Examine the Quranic argument
Proponents of darkness as a positive quality cite Quran 6:1. See how Al-Taftazani refutes this by distinguishing 'pure non-existence' from 'specific non-existence'.
Specific non-existence, like blindness, is attributable to God's act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهو عدم الضوء عما شأنه الضوء بالكلية وينعدم، وهو ملكة للضوء لا كيفية وجودية على ما ذهب إليه البعض، وإلا لكان مانعاً للجالس في الغار (٤) من إبصار من هو في هواء مضيء خارج الغـار، كما أنه مانع له من إبصار من هو في الغار (٥) ، وذلك للقطع بعدم الفرق في الحائل المانع من الإبصار بين أن يكون محيطاً بالرائي والمرئي، أو متوسطاً بينهما، وربما يمنع ذلك بأنه ليس بمانع بل إحاطة الضوء بالمرئي شرط للرؤية وهو منتف في الغار، لكنه لا يتأتى على قولهم الظلمة كيفية مانعة من الإبصار تمسك القائلون بكونها وجودية بقوله تعالى : وجعل الظلمات والنور فإن المجعول لا يكون إلا موجوداً . وأجيب بالمنع ، فإن الجاعل كما يجعل الوجود يجعل العدم الخاص، کالعمى فإنما المنافي للمجعولية هو العدم الصرف.
So it (darkness) is the absence of light from that which is by nature light, and it is entirely absent, and it is a disposition (malaka) for light, not a positive quality (kayfiyya wujūdiyya), according to what some held. Otherwise, it would be a barrier for the one sitting in the cave from seeing someone in luminous air outside the cave, just as it is a barrier for him from seeing someone in the cave, and that because of the certainty that there is no difference in the intervening barrier preventing sight whether it surrounds the seer and the seen, or is intermediate between them. And perhaps one may refute this by saying that it is not a barrier; rather, the surrounding of light around the visible is a condition for vision, and it is absent in the cave, but this does not follow from their statement that darkness is a quality preventing sight. Those who affirm it to be positive hold to His saying: 'And He made the darknesses and the light' (Quran 6:1) – for what is made cannot be but existent. And it was answered by denial: for the maker, just as He makes existence, He makes the specific non-existence, like blindness; indeed, what is incompatible with being made is pure non-existence.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage argues that darkness is not a positive quality but a privation of light. The author begins by stating that darkness is the absence of light and a disposition for light. He then presents an argument: if darkness were a positive quality, it would block vision for a person in a cave seeing someone outside, but it is known that the barrier does not differ whether it surrounds or is intermediate. A possible objection is raised: the barrier is not darkness but lack of light surrounding the visible. However, this objection does not hold if darkness is a positive quality. Supporters of the positive view cite Quran 6:1 ('He made the darknesses and the light'), arguing that 'made' implies existence. The response is that while pure non-existence cannot be made, specific non-existence (like blindness) can be attributed to God's act. Thus, the verse does not require darkness to be a positive existent.

Misconception: The Quranic verse 'He made the darknesses and the light' proves darkness is a positive existent entity.
Correction: The verse is reconciled by noting that God can create specific non-existence (like blindness), so darkness can be a created privation, not a positive quality.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Darkness is a created privation of light, not an existent quality; even Quranic creation language can refer to specific non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Do you also acknowledge that these are created by Him as specific non-existences, and that He could replace them with light if He willed? How does that change your supplication today?
?

?

Are you willing to ask God to bring the light, rather than just curse the darkness?
