Definition of Perception: Presence as the Core
Al-Taftazani defines perception as presence and divides it into intellectual and sensory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Taftazani's definition of perception as the presence (ḥuḍūr) of a form, and its division into intellectual and sensory perception.

Core Idea
Perception is not about external existence; it is the presence of a form in the perceiver.

In this passage, al-Taftazani lays out the foundational definition of perception. He explains that the term 'observation' (mushāhada) means mere presence, not visual sight. By distinguishing between intellectual and sensory perception, he sets the stage for a deeper understanding of how we come to know things.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key terms: mushāhada, ḥuḍūr, idrāk, ṣūra, irtisām, āla.
Pay attention to the phrase 'مطلق الحضور' (absolute presence).
2
Identify the two divisions of perception
The passage states that perception is divided into that which occurs without an instrument (intellectual) and that which occurs with an instrument (sensory). Find examples for each.
Sensory perception uses a sense organ like the eye or common sense.
3
Understand the repetition in expression
Al-Taftazani notes that in intellectual perception there is a verbal repetition, and in sensory perception there is a semantic repetition. Ask why this is significant for the definition.
The repetition hints at the unique nature of intellectual vs. sensory presence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المثال منتزعاً من أمر خارج أو متحصلا ابتداء وسواء كان منطبع في ذات المدرك، أو في آلته والمراد بالمشاهدة مطلق الحضور، وفي قوله يشاهدها مابه يدرك تنبيه على انقسام الإدراك إلى ما يكون بغير آلة فيكون ارتسام الصورة في ذات المدرك، وإلى ما يكون بآلة فيكون في محل الحس، كما في الإبصار بحصول الصورة في الرطوبة الجليدية، أو في المجاور كإدراك الحس المشترك بحصول الصورة الخيالية في محل متصل به والمراد بالمشاهدة مجرد الحضور على ما هو معناها اللغوي لا الإبصار وإدراك عين الشيء الخارجي على ما هو المتعارف ليلزم فساد التفسير، نعم تضمنت العبارة في جانب الإدراك العقلي تكراراً بحسب اللفظ كأنه قيل حضور عند المدرك حال الحضور عنده لأن ما به الإدراك العقلي هو ذات المدرك، وفي جانب الإدراك الحسي تكرار بحسب المعنى حتى كأن هناك حضورين أحدهما عند المدرك والآخر عند الآلة وليس كذلك بل الحضور عند النفس هو الحضور عند الحس وتحقيق المقام أنا إذا أدركنا شيئاً فلا خفاء في أنه يحصل لنا حال لم تكن وتكاد تشهد الفطرة بأنها بحصول أمر لم يكن لا بزوال أمر كان وما ذاك إلا تميزاً وظهوراً لذلك الشيء عند العقل، وليس ذلك بوجوده في الخارج إذ كثيراً ما ندرك ما لا وجود له في الخارج من المعدومات بل الممتنعات، وكثيراً ما يوجد الشيء في الخارج ولا يدركه العقل مع تشوقه إليه، بل بوجوده في العقل بمعنى أن يحصل فيه أثر يناسب ذلك الشيء بحيث لو وجد في الخارج لكان إياه، وهذا هو المعنى بحصول الصورة وحضورها وتمثلها وارتسامها ووصول النفس إليها، ونحو ذلك، ولا يفهم من إدراك الشيء سواه والاعتراض بأن الإدراك صفة المدرك والحصول ونحوه صفة الصورة مما لا يلتفت إليه عند المحققين سواء جعلنا الإدراك مصدراً بمعنى الفاعل أو المفعول، وأما الاعتراض بأن ذكر المدرك وما به يدرك في تعريف الإدراك دور، فجوابه أن المراد به الشيء الذي قال له: المدرك وما به الإدراك، وإن لم تعرف حقيقة هذا الوصف، وقد يجاب بأن هذا ليس تعريفاً للإدراك، بل تعييناً وتلخيصاً للمعنى المسمى بالإدراك الواضح عند العقل.
The example is abstracted from an external matter or obtained initially, whether it is impressed in the essence of the perceiver or in its instrument. The meaning of 'observation' (mushāhada) is absolute presence (ḥuḍūr). In his statement 'he observes it by that which perceives', there is an indication of the division of perception (idrāk) into that which is without an instrument, so the impression (irtisām) of the form (ṣūra) is in the essence of the perceiver, and into that which is with an instrument, so it is in the locus of the sense (ḥiss), as in vision by the occurrence of the form in the icy moisture, or in the adjacent as the perception of the common sense by the occurrence of the imaginary form in a locus connected to it. The intended meaning of 'observation' is mere presence according to its linguistic meaning, not vision (ibṣār) and perception of the external thing itself as is customary, lest the interpretation become corrupt. Indeed, the expression contains a repetition in the aspect of intellectual perception (idrāk 'aqlī) in terms of wording, as if it were said: presence at the perceiver at the time of presence at him, because that by which intellectual perception occurs is the essence of the perceiver. In the aspect of sensory perception (idrāk ḥissī), there is a repetition in terms of meaning, such that there seem to be two presences: one at the perceiver and the other at the instrument, but it is not so; rather, presence at the soul (nafs) is presence at the sense. The verification of the situation is that when we perceive something, there is no doubt that we obtain a state that did not exist, and innate disposition (fiṭra) almost testifies that it is by the occurrence of something that was not, not by the removal of something that was. That is nothing but a distinctness and manifestation of that thing to the intellect. That is not by its existence in externality, since we often perceive what has no existence in externality, such as non-existents (ma'dūmāt) and even impossibilities (mumtani'āt), and often a thing exists in externality but the intellect does not perceive it despite its desire for it. Rather, it is by its existence in the intellect, meaning that an effect suitable to that thing occurs in it, such that if it existed externally, it would be that thing. This is the meaning of the occurrence of the form, its presence, its representation (tamaththul), its impression (irtisām), and the soul's reaching it, and similar expressions. Nothing else is understood from the perception of the thing. The objection that perception is an attribute of the perceiver and occurrence and the like are attributes of the form is not heeded by the verifiers (muḥaqqiqūn), whether we take perception (idrāk) as a verbal noun in the sense of the agent or the object. As for the objection that mentioning the perceiver and that by which it perceives in the definition of perception is circular (dawr), the answer is that what is meant is the thing that is called the perceiver and that by which perception occurs, even if the reality of this description is not known. And it may also be answered that this is not a definition of perception, but rather a designation and summary of the meaning called perception that is clear to the intellect.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage defines perception (idrāk) and addresses a key objection. It begins by clarifying that perception involves the presence (ḥuḍūr) of a form (ṣūra) in the perceiver, either directly in the essence (dhāt) or via an instrument (āla). The author distinguishes intellectual perception (idrāk 'aqlī) from sensory perception (idrāk ḥissī). In intellectual perception, the form impresses directly on the mind, leading to a verbal repetition in the phrase 'presence at the perceiver at the time of presence'. In sensory perception, there is a conceptual repetition because there appear to be two presences—one at the soul and one at the sense organ—but actually the soul's presence is the sense's presence. The author then explains the reality of perception: when we perceive, a new state occurs, which is a distinctness and manifestation of the thing to the intellect, not its external existence. We can perceive non-existents and impossibilities, and external objects may go unperceived. Perception is the occurrence in the intellect of an effect corresponding to the thing, i.e., the form (ṣūra). The objection that perception is an attribute of the perceiver while the form is an attribute of the perceived is dismissed as irrelevant to the verifiers. Finally, the objection of circularity (dawr) in defining perception by mentioning the perceiver and the means is answered in two ways: first, the definition refers to the thing's common understanding even if its reality is unknown; second, it is not a strict definition but a designation of a clear concept.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary meaning of 'observation' (mushāhada) in the definition of perception?
A) Vision (ibṣār) of the external object
B) Sense perception of material things
C) Absolute presence (ḥuḍūr) in its linguistic sense
D) Intellectual comprehension of a form

Key Takeaway
Perception is the presence of a form in the perceiver, either directly (intellectual) or via a sense organ.

The Circularity Objection and Its Resolution
Al-Taftazani verifies perception as mental presence and answers the circularity objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Taftazani's verification of perception as a new state of manifestation in the intellect, his dismissal of the attribute objection, and his two responses to the circularity charge.

Core Idea
Perception is a distinctness in the intellect, not external existence; the definition's reference to perceiver and means does not cause circularity.

Having defined perception as presence, al-Taftazani now defends this definition. He first verifies that perception is a new state of manifestation, distinct from the object's external existence. Then he dismisses a weak objection and tackles the more serious charge of circularity (dawr) in the definition. His answers illustrate the subtlety of Islamic epistemological analysis.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the verification of perception
Read the section beginning with 'وتحقيق المقام'. Al-Taftazani argues that perception is a new state (ḥāl) of distinctness and manifestation, not caused by the object's external existence since we can perceive non-existents and impossibilities.
He emphasizes that perception is the occurrence of a suitable effect in the intellect.
2
Understand the dismissal of the attribute objection
The objection that perception is an attribute of the perceiver while the form is an attribute of the perceived is dismissed as irrelevant to verifiers.
This objection does not undermine the definition.
3
Examine the circularity objection and its answers
The charge is that defining perception by mentioning 'the perceiver' and 'that by which it perceives' is circular. Al-Taftazani gives two answers: (1) the definition refers to the thing's common understanding, (2) it is not a strict definition but a designation.
The second answer is often preferred by scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المثال منتزعاً من أمر خارج أو متحصلا ابتداء وسواء كان منطبع في ذات المدرك، أو في آلته والمراد بالمشاهدة مطلق الحضور، وفي قوله يشاهدها مابه يدرك تنبيه على انقسام الإدراك إلى ما يكون بغير آلة فيكون ارتسام الصورة في ذات المدرك، وإلى ما يكون بآلة فيكون في محل الحس، كما في الإبصار بحصول الصورة في الرطوبة الجليدية، أو في المجاور كإدراك الحس المشترك بحصول الصورة الخيالية في محل متصل به والمراد بالمشاهدة مجرد الحضور على ما هو معناها اللغوي لا الإبصار وإدراك عين الشيء الخارجي على ما هو المتعارف ليلزم فساد التفسير، نعم تضمنت العبارة في جانب الإدراك العقلي تكراراً بحسب اللفظ كأنه قيل حضور عند المدرك حال الحضور عنده لأن ما به الإدراك العقلي هو ذات المدرك، وفي جانب الإدراك الحسي تكرار بحسب المعنى حتى كأن هناك حضورين أحدهما عند المدرك والآخر عند الآلة وليس كذلك بل الحضور عند النفس هو الحضور عند الحس وتحقيق المقام أنا إذا أدركنا شيئاً فلا خفاء في أنه يحصل لنا حال لم تكن وتكاد تشهد الفطرة بأنها بحصول أمر لم يكن لا بزوال أمر كان وما ذاك إلا تميزاً وظهوراً لذلك الشيء عند العقل، وليس ذلك بوجوده في الخارج إذ كثيراً ما ندرك ما لا وجود له في الخارج من المعدومات بل الممتنعات، وكثيراً ما يوجد الشيء في الخارج ولا يدركه العقل مع تشوقه إليه، بل بوجوده في العقل بمعنى أن يحصل فيه أثر يناسب ذلك الشيء بحيث لو وجد في الخارج لكان إياه، وهذا هو المعنى بحصول الصورة وحضورها وتمثلها وارتسامها ووصول النفس إليها، ونحو ذلك، ولا يفهم من إدراك الشيء سواه والاعتراض بأن الإدراك صفة المدرك والحصول ونحوه صفة الصورة مما لا يلتفت إليه عند المحققين سواء جعلنا الإدراك مصدراً بمعنى الفاعل أو المفعول، وأما الاعتراض بأن ذكر المدرك وما به يدرك في تعريف الإدراك دور، فجوابه أن المراد به الشيء الذي قال له: المدرك وما به الإدراك، وإن لم تعرف حقيقة هذا الوصف، وقد يجاب بأن هذا ليس تعريفاً للإدراك، بل تعييناً وتلخيصاً للمعنى المسمى بالإدراك الواضح عند العقل.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That perception is the same as the external existence of the perceived object.
Correction: Perception is a mental presence (ḥuḍūr dhihnī) independent of external reality; we perceive non-existents and impossibilities, and external objects may remain unperceived.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a correct answer to the circularity objection?
A) The definition refers to the common understanding of 'perceiver' and 'means'.
B) The definition is not a strict definition but a designation of a clear concept.
C) The definition is circular because it uses the term 'perception' in the definiens.
D) The definition merely summarizes a meaning already clear to the intellect.

Key Takeaway
Perception is not external existence but a mental manifestation; the definition's use of 'perceiver' and 'means' is either based on common understanding or is merely a designation, avoiding circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you confused a mental assumption about someone's intentions for an external reality? How would your actions change if you consciously distinguished between the mental presence of your suspicion and the actual state of affairs?
?
Al-Taftazani uses ordinary language to define perception. In your own life, do you demand airtight proofs for matters of faith, or do you accept clear meanings already present in your heart and intellect? Are you willing to admit that sometimes naming is enough?
?
The author says we perceive impossibilities. There are things you consider impossible about God's mercy or your own capacity for change. Could your mind's 'impossibility' actually be a mental presence you are afraid to fully engage? What would happen if you took it as a sign of your own limitation, not of God's?

Classification of Perceived Objects: Internal and External
Al-Taftazani classifies perceived objects as internal or external with three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani classifies perceived objects into internal (perceived by essence) and external (material vs immaterial), and the corresponding forms of perception.

Core Idea
Perception is either of the self by essence, or of externals by abstraction or direct representation.

In this passage, al-Taftazani presents a foundational classification for epistemology in Islamic theology. He distinguishes between objects perceived by their very essence (like the soul's self-awareness) and external objects, which are further divided into material and immaterial. This taxonomy clarifies how different types of knowledge correspond to different modes of perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall structure.
Notice that the passage includes both the matn (bold) and the sharh (explanation).
2
Identify the three categories
The passage presents three types of perceived objects: non-external (perceived by essence), external material (abstracted form), and external immaterial/non-existent (direct representation). Find and note each.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why self-perception does not require abstraction and why immaterial objects are known directly. Think about how this classification avoids infinite regress.
This connects to the broader kalam concern about the nature of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو المدرك إما أن يكون خارجاً أو غير خارج (قال: أما بحقيقته كإدراك النفس ذاتها وصفاتها فيكون التغاير اعتبارياً كاف كالمعالج يعالج نفسه، ومثله العلم بالعلم فلا يلزم وجود ما لا نهاية وإما بصورتها المنتزعة كما في الماديات أو غير المنتزعة كما في المجردات والمعدومات). إشارة إلى ما ذكروا من أن الشيء المدرك إما أن لا يكون خارجاً عن ذات المدرك كالنفس وصفاتها، وإما أن يكون خارجاً وحينئذ فإما أن يكون مادياً أو غير مادي. فالأول تكون حقيقته المتمثلة عند المدرك نفس حقيقته الموجودة في الخارج فيكون إدراكه دائماً. والثاني: تكون صورة منتزعة عنه. والثالث: تكون صورة متحصلة في العقل غير مفتقرة إلى الانتزاع من حقيقة خارجية، لكونها صورة لما هو مجرد في نفسه كإدراك المفارقات، أو لما لا تحقق له في الخارج ولا حقيقة أصلاً كإدراك المعلومات.
And the perceived object is either external or not external. (He said: [It is perceived] either by its essence, like the soul's (nafs) perception of itself and its attributes, so the distinction is conceptual (i‘tibārī) and sufficient, like the patient treating himself, and similarly knowledge of knowledge, so an infinite regress does not arise; or by its abstracted form (ṣūra muntaza‘a) as in material things, or by a non-abstracted form as in immaterial things (mujarradāt) and non-existents (ma‘dūmāt).) This is a reference to what they mentioned: that the perceived thing either is not external to the essence of the perceiver, like the soul and its attributes, or it is external, and then either material (mādī) or immaterial (ghayr mādī). The first: its reality represented at the perceiver is the same as its reality existing externally, so its perception is perpetual. The second: a form abstracted from it. The third: a form obtained in the intellect (dhihn) without needing abstraction from an external reality, because it is a form of what is immaterial in itself, like perception of separate substances (mufāriqāt), or of what has no actualization in externality and no reality at all, like perception of mental objects (ma‘lūmāt).
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of perceived objects. The author, quoting the matn, states that the perceived object (al-mudrak) is either external (khārij) or not external (ghayr khārij). If it is not external, it is perceived by its very essence, as when the soul (nafs) perceives itself and its attributes; here the distinction between perceiver and perceived is merely conceptual (i‘tibārī) and sufficient, like a patient treating himself (the same entity is both agent and patient). Similarly, knowledge of knowledge does not lead to an infinite regress. If the perceived object is external, it is either material (mādī) or immaterial (ghayr mādī). For material things, the form is abstracted (muntaza‘a) from the external object. For immaterial things (like separate intellects) and non-existents, the form is obtained in the intellect without needing abstraction—it is a direct mental representation. The explanation (sharh) then elaborates: when the perceived object is not external (like the soul), its reality as represented is identical to its external reality, so its perception is perpetual. When it is external material, the form is an abstraction. When it is external immaterial or non-existent, the form is a mental acquisition not requiring abstraction.

Key Terms
مدرك
خارج
ذات
نفس
صورة
منتزعة
مجرد
مادي
مفارقات
معلومات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All perception of external objects requires abstraction from an external reality.
Correction: The passage shows that immaterial objects and non-existents are perceived directly without abstraction, as their forms are already in the intellect.

Checkpoint
According to the classification, what kind of form is involved in perceiving the soul's own attributes?
A) An abstracted form (ṣūra muntaza‘a)
B) A non-abstracted form (ṣūra ghayr muntaza‘a)
C) The same reality (ḥaqīqa) as the essence itself
D) A mental form obtained through abstraction

Key Takeaway
The classification distinguishes three modes of perception: self-perception by essence, material perception by abstraction, and immaterial perception by direct mental representation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The soul's self-perception is described as perpetual. When do you most clearly experience this awareness of your own existence? How does this constant self-knowledge challenge you to live more intentionally, knowing you are always present to yourself before God?
?
The passage distinguishes between perceiving material objects through abstraction and immaterial realities directly. When you reflect on your own soul—an immaterial reality—do you treat it as something to be abstracted from physical experience, or do you seek direct knowledge of it? What would change if you prioritized direct self-awareness over external distractions?

First Objection to Perpetual Self-Perception
Al-Taftazani presents the first objection against perpetual self-perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection raised against the claim that the soul's self-perception is perpetual.

Core Idea
Perpetual self-perception would logically require awareness of all attributes, but we are unaware of many.

Al-Taftazani begins listing objections to the idea that the soul (nafs) perpetually perceives itself. The first objection attacks the logical consequence: if the soul is always present to itself, then it must know all its attributes at all times. Yet experience shows we are unaware of many of our own attributes, so the premise must be flawed.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice that it is the first of several objections (وجوه). The argument is brief but concise.
The passage cuts off; the full objection and response may continue later.
2
Identify the logical structure
The objection uses a conditional: if perpetual presence (حضور) then perpetual knowledge (دوام إدراك). The consequent is false (لازم باطل), so the antecedent must be false. Understand this reductio ad absurdum.
3
Reflect on the premise of 'presence'
The objection assumes that 'presence' of the self to itself would guarantee knowledge. Ask yourself: is presence sufficient for knowledge? This is the key point of contention.
The response (not given here) might challenge that premise.
4
Consider the force of the objection
Think about whether you agree with the objection. Does experience indeed show that we are ignorant of many of our attributes? This is an empirical claim that could be debated.
The objection is grounded in common experience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض على الأول بوجوه: أحدها: أنه يقتضي أن يكون إدراك النفس لذاتها وصفاتها دائماً لدوام الحضور، واللازم باطل لأن كثيراً من الصفات مما لا نطلع على
And objections were raised against the first (claim) with several points: One of them: that it necessitates that the soul's (nafs) perception of itself and its attributes be perpetual due to the perpetuity of presence, and the consequent is false because many of the attributes are not known to us.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection to the claim that the soul's perception of itself and its attributes is perpetual (dā'im). The objection argues: if the soul always has presence (ḥuḍūr) of itself, then it must always perceive all of its attributes. But that consequence is false because we are unaware of many of our own attributes. The objection is cut off before the response, but it challenges the idea that self-knowledge is constant and complete.

Correction: The objection shows that this is false because we are not aware of many of our own attributes; presence does not guarantee exhaustive knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the first objection, why cannot the soul's self-perception be perpetual?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Ibn Sina's Definition of Health
Definition of health by Ibn Sina with commentary on its nuances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's definition of health and the key distinctions made in the commentary regarding its genus, fixity, scope, and linguistic interpretation.

Core Idea
Health is a disposition from which actions issue soundly, not a mere absence of disease.

This lesson examines Ibn Sina's foundational definition of health, which serves as the basis for discussions in medicine and theology. The commentary clarifies nuances about fixity (whether it must be a fixed disposition), scope (whether it applies to plants or only humans), and avoids circular definitions by appealing to the linguistic meaning of health and safety.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with 'Among them are health and illness' — indicating a list.
2
Identify the core definition and its genus
Find Ibn Sina's definition: health is a disposition (malaka) or state (ḥāl) from which actions issue soundly. Note that its genus is psychic quality (kayfiyya nafsāniyya).
3
Consider the critiques of alternative views
The passage criticizes those who restrict health to fixed dispositions only, and the Imām (al-Rāzī) for including plants. Understand why these are problematic according to the author.
The critique about plants highlights that plants lack disposition/state in the relevant sense.
4
Understand the linguistic argument and avoidance of circularity
The author argues that 'health' and 'safety' are taken in their linguistic meaning when attributed to actions, thus avoiding a vicious circle. Also examine the discussion of the prepositions 'an and min indicating principiality.
This step is more advanced; focus on the overall point that the definition is not circular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: منها الصحة والمرض. أما الصحة فعرفها ابن سينا بأنها ملكة أو حال تصدر عنها الأفعال الموضوع لها سليمة يعني أن جنسها الكيفية النفسانية سواء كانت بصفة الرسوخ أو دونها لا كما هو رأي البعض من تخصيصها بالراسخة على ما قال في الشفاء ملكة في الجسم الحيواني تصدر عنه لأجلها الأفعال غير فارقة، وقدم الملكة لأنها أشرف وأغلب والمتفق على كونها صحة، وفيما زعم الإمام من شمولها صحة النبات ذهول عن معنى الملكة والحالة، وأما تخصيصها بالإنسان فيما قال : إنها هيئة يكون بها بدن الإنسان في مزاجه وتركيبه بحيث يصدر عنه الأفعال كلها صحيحة سليمة، فبالنظر إلى أنها المبحوث عنها في الطب، والمراد بالصحة والسلامة المعنى اللغوي بدليل الإسناد إلى الأفعال فلا دور . والدلالة بكلمتي عن ومن على مبدئية كل من الحال والمحل مبنية على أن
He said: Among them are health and illness. As for health, Ibn Sina defined it as a disposition (malaka) or state (ḥāl) from which the actions of the subject issue soundly, meaning that its genus is psychic quality (kayfiyya nafsāniyya), whether with the attribute of fixity or below it, not as is the opinion of some who restrict it to the fixed, as he said in the Shifā': a disposition in the animal body from which actions issue on account of it, without differentiation. And he gave precedence to 'disposition' because it is nobler, more prevalent, and unanimously agreed to be health. And in what the Imām claimed of its including the health of plants, there is heedlessness of the meaning of disposition and state. As for restricting it to humans in what he said: it is a form (hay'a) by which the human body is in its temperament (mizāj) and composition (tarkīb) such that all its actions issue soundly and safely, then it is with regard to it being the subject of investigation in medicine. And by health and safety is meant the linguistic meaning, evidenced by the attribution to actions, so there is no vicious circle. And the indication by the two words 'an (from) and min (from) of the principiality of both the state and the locus is based on that...
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of health (ṣiḥḥa) as given by Ibn Sina, with commentary. The author states that Ibn Sina defined health as a disposition (malaka) or state (ḥāl) from which the actions of the subject (the body) proceed in a sound manner. The genus of health is said to be psychic quality (kayfiyya nafsāniyya), which can be either fixed (rāsikha) or not, contrary to some who restrict it to fixed states only. Ibn Sina in the Shifā' defined it as a disposition in the animal body from which actions proceed without differentiation. The author notes that 'disposition' is preferred because it is nobler, more common, and universally agreed to be health. The author criticizes the Imām (likely al-Rāzī) for extending health to plants, arguing that this ignores the meaning of disposition and state. The definition is then restricted to humans because it is the subject of medicine. The author clarifies that the terms 'health' and 'safety' refer to their linguistic meanings, as evidenced by their attribution to actions, thus avoiding a circular definition. The passage ends by discussing the prepositions 'an and min indicating the principiality of both the state and its locus.

Key Terms

ملكة (malaka)
حال (ḥāl)
كيفية نفسانية (kayfiyya nafsāniyya)
هيئة (hay'a)
مزاج (mizāj)
تركيب (tarkīb)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Health is merely the absence of disease or must be a fixed disposition.
Correction: Health is defined as a disposition (malaka) or state (ḥāl) from which actions issue soundly, and it can be either fixed or non-fixed.

Checkpoint
Why does the author criticize the Imām's inclusion of plant health in the definition?
A) Because plants do not have souls.
B) Because health of plants is not a disposition or state.
C) Because plants are not studied in medicine.
D) Because plants do not have actions.

Key Takeaway
Health is defined as a disposition or state from which actions issue soundly, and this definition avoids circularity by using the linguistic meaning of health and safety.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The passage warns against restricting health only to fixed states. Is there an area of your life where you feel your spiritual state is 'non-fixed' and you therefore conclude you are unhealthy? ?
The author argues that defining health by sound actions is not circular because the terms are used linguistically. Health Definition and Circularity Objection
Examines Ibn Sina's definitions of health, the objection of circularity, and two responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's definition of health, the objection that it is circular, and how two responses resolve it.

Core Idea
Health is defined by its effects, not by itself.

Ibn Sina's definition of health in his works al-Shifa and al-Qanun sparked a debate about circularity. The objection claimed that defining health by sound actions defines the thing by itself. This lesson examines the definitions, the objection, and two sophisticated responses that clarify the causal relationship between health and its effects.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions from al-Shifa and al-Qanun
Read the Arabic passage and translation to see how Ibn Sina defines health in both works. Note the phrasing difference.
The definition involves health as a quality from which sound actions proceed.
2
Identify the objection of circularity
Look for the objection that the definition defines the thing by itself. The objection stems from the phrase 'actions proceed from it' which might confuse the principle and the subject.
3
Understand the two responses
The first response treats health as an efficient principle and the subject as receptive. The second makes health an intermediary cause. Compare both.
The fire analogy clarifies the second response.
4
Follow the verification and conclusion
Read how the author verifies the second response using the fire analogy and concludes that the objection only applies to the first definition, not the second.
Notice the role of the letter لام vs باء in clarifying causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنما تكونان من الكيفيات النفسانية، أي المختصة بذوات الأنفس الحيوانية على ما صرحوا به، وعلى هذا يكون في تعريف الشفاء تكرار اللهم إلا أن يراد بالملكة الحال الراسخ وغير الراسخ من مطلق الكيفية، أو يراد بالأنفس أعم من الحيوانية والنباتية وكلاهما خلاف الاصطلاح.
وأما ما ذكر في موضع آخر من القانون أن الصحة هيئة يكون بها بدن الإنسان في مزاجه وتركيبه بحيث تصدر عنه الأفعال كلها صحيحة سليمة، فمبني على أن الصحة المبحوث عنها في الطب هي صحة الإنسان والمراد بصحة الأفعال وسلامتها خلوصها عن الآفة وكونها على المجرى الطبيعي على ما يناسب المعنى اللغوي فلا يكون تعريف صحة البدن والعضوبها تعريف الشيء بنفسه وهذا ما قال الإمام : : أن الصحة في الأفعال أمر محسوس وفي البدن غير محسوس، وتعریف غير محسوس بالمحسوس جائز وأما الاعتراض بأن قوله: تصدر عنها الأفعال مشعر بأن المبدأ هي تلك الملكة أو الحال وقوله : من الموضوع مشعر بأنه الموضوع أعني البدن أو العضو.
فأجيب عنه بوجهين :
أحدهما أن الصحة مبدأ فاعلي، والموضوع قابل والمعنى كيفية تصدر عنها الأفعال الكائنة من الموضوع الحاصلة فيه . وثانيهما: أن الموضوع قابل والصحة واسطة بمنزلة العلة لفاعليته والمعنى يصدر لأجلها وبواسطتها الأفعال من الموضوع وتحقيق أن القوى الجسمانية لا تصدر عنها أفعالها إلا بشركة من موضوعاتها، فالمسخن هو النار والنارية علة لكون النار مسخنة. فالمراد أن الصحة علة لصيرورة البدن مصدراً للفعل السليم. وهذا المعنى واضح في عبارة القانون في التعريف الثاني، وأوضح منه عبارة الشفاء لأن اللام أوضح في التعليل من الباء، وهي من عن فاندفاع الاعتراض عنها في غاية الظهور والإمام إنما أورده على العبارة الأولى فما ذكر في المواقف أن الصحة ملكة أو حالة تصدر بها الأفعال عن الموضوع لها سليمة . وأن الإمام أورد عليه هذا الاعتراض ليس على ما ينبغي
They (health and disease) are only of the psychic qualities, i.e., those specific to animal souls as they have explained. On this, there is repetition in the definition of al-Shifa, unless by 'malaka' is meant the firmly rooted state and the non-firmly rooted from the absolute quality, or by 'souls' is meant more general than animal and vegetative, both of which are against convention. As for what is mentioned in another place of al-Qanun that health is a disposition by which the human body is in its temperament and structure such that all actions proceed from it sound and healthy, it is based on the fact that health discussed in medicine is human health, and by the soundness and healthiness of actions is meant their purity from defect and their being upon the natural course according to what suits the linguistic meaning, so that the definition of the health of the body and its limb is not a definition of the thing by itself. And this is what the Imam said: that health in actions is perceptible and in the body is imperceptible, and defining the imperceptible by the perceptible is permissible. As for the objection that his saying 'actions proceed from it' indicates that the principle is that malaka or state, and his saying 'from the subject' indicates that it is the subject, i.e., the body or limb. It was answered with two aspects: One: that health is an efficient principle, and the subject is receptive, and the meaning is: a quality from which the actions occurring from the subject and taking place in it proceed. Second: that the subject is receptive and health is an intermediary akin to a cause for its efficiency, and the meaning is: for its sake and through its mediation the actions proceed from the subject. And verification that bodily powers do not produce their actions except with participation from their subjects, so the heater is fire, and the 'fireness' is the cause for fire being a heater. So the intended meaning is that health is a cause for the body becoming a source for sound action. This meaning is clear in the expression of al-Qanun in the second definition, and clearer than it is the expression of al-Shifa because the لام is clearer for causation than the باء, and it is 'from'? So the objection against it is repelled very clearly. And the Imam only raised it against the first expression. So what is mentioned in al-Mawaqif that health is a malaka or state by which actions proceed from the subject of it soundly, and that the Imam raised this objection against it, is not as it should be.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage discusses Ibn Sina's definition of health, beginning by noting that the definitions in al-Shifa and al-Qanun may appear repetitive. The author explains the potential repetition in the definition of health as a quality causing sound actions, but offers two possible resolutions: either 'malaka' includes both firm and non-firm states, or 'souls' includes animal and vegetative, though these are non-standard. The text then presents the definition from al-Qanun: health is a disposition enabling the body to perform all actions soundly. This definition avoids circularity because it defines bodily health in terms of perceptible sound actions, which is permissible. An objection arises: the phrase 'actions proceed from it' implies the principle is the malaka, and 'from the subject' implies the subject is the body or limb, potentially causing confusion. Two answers are given: first, health is an efficient principle and the subject is receptive, so the quality originates from health and occurs in the subject; second, health is an intermediary cause for the subject's efficiency, similar to fire being a cause for heating. The author concludes that the second definition in al-Qanun is clear, and the objection only applies to the first definition in al-Shifa. The passage ends by criticizing a claim in al-Mawaqif that the Imam raised an objection against a definition that he did not actually object to.

ملكة
حال
كيفية
مبدأ فاعلي
موضوع قابل
العلة
الشريفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Defining health by sound actions is circular because it defines health by itself.
Correction: The definition is not circular because health is imperceptible and is known by its perceptible effects (sound actions), which is a valid definition of an imperceptible state.

Checkpoint
What is the primary objection raised against the definition of health in al-Shifa?
A) It defines health by itself (circularity).
B) It confuses the efficient principle with the subject.
C) It uses imperceptible qualities.
D) It omits the role of the soul.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's definition of health is not circular because it defines an imperceptible state by its perceptible effects, and the two responses clarify the causal relationship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you assess your own health, do you rely on how you feel internally or on external signs like your ability to perform daily tasks? How does this lesson's distinction between imperceptible health and perceptible actions challenge you to be more honest about your actual state? Commit to one observable action you will use as a gauge for your health this week.
?
The passage warns against circular definitions. In your own spiritual life, do you define yourself as 'faithful' simply because you perform rituals, without examining the inner disposition that should produce those actions? What one specific change would break that circular thinking?
?
Ibn Sina's definition treats health as a cause that makes the body a source of sound action. Consider a relationship you have—are you the source of sound actions (like patience or kindness) because of a healthy inner state, or do you rely on external validation? Ibn Sina's Two Definitions of Disease and Their Logical Opposition
Ibn Sina's definition and the logical analysis of health-disease opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Sina's two definitions of disease lead to two different logical relationships between health and disease: privative opposition and contrary opposition.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's nuanced view: disease as privation or as a harmful state determines its logical opposition to health.

This passage from the Sharh al-Maqasid presents a key discussion in Islamic medicine and philosophy: the precise definition of disease. Ibn Sina's two seemingly conflicting statements — one defining disease as a contrary disposition, the other as pure non-existence — are reconciled by distinguishing two senses of the term. The analysis reveals that the logical relationship between health and disease depends on which sense is intended.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation, paying attention to the two statements by Ibn Sina and the reconciliation.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find where Ibn Sina defines disease as a contrary disposition (malaka) and where he calls it pure non-existence. Note the implications for the type of opposition with health.
3
Understand the reconciliation
The author reconciles the two definitions by showing that disease can refer to the absence of the health disposition (privative) or to the presence of a harmful disposition (contrary). Grasp this distinction.
This is the core of the lesson.
4
Distinguish technical and common usage
Note that in technical investigation (عرف خاص), the opposition is privative, while in common usage (عرف عام), it is contrary. The example of even and odd numbers illustrates this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المرض فقد عرفه ابن سينا بأنه هيئة مضادة للصحة أي ملكة أو حالة تصدر عنها الأفعال عن الموضوع لها غير سليمة، وذكر في موضع من الشفاء أن المرض من حيث هو مرض بالحقيقة فهو عدم محض لست أعني من حيث هو سوء مزاج أو ألم ، وهذا مشعر بأن بينهما تقابل الملكة والعدم، ووجه التوفيق بين كلاميه على ما أشار إليه الإمام، هو أن الصحة عنده هيئة هي مبدأ لسلامة الأفعال وعند المرض تزول تلك الهيئة، وتحدث هيئة هي مبدأ للآفة في الأفعال، فإن جعل المرض عبارة عن عدم الهيئة الأولى وزوالها فبينهما تقابل العدم والملكة ، وإن جعل عبارة عن نفس الهيئة الثانية فتقابل التضاد، وكأنه يريد أن لفظ المرض مشترك بين الأمرين أو حقيقة في أحدهما، مجاز في الآخر، وإلا فالإشكال بحاله . وقيل المراد أن بينهما تقابل العدم والملكة بحسب التحقيق وهو العرف الخاص على ما مر أو تقابل التضاد بحسب الشهرة، وهو العرف العامي لأن المشهور أن الضدين أمران ينسبان إلى موضوع واحد ولا يمكن أن يجتمعا كالزوجية والفردية لا بحسب التحقيق ليلزم كونهما موجودين في غاية التخلف تحت جنس واحد قريب وقد صرح بذلك
As for disease, Ibn Sina defined it as a disposition contrary to health, i.e., a malaka or state by which actions proceed from its subject unsoundly. And he mentioned in a place of al-Shifa that disease, inasmuch as it is disease in reality, is pure non-existence, not meaning inasmuch as it is bad temper or pain. And this indicates that between them is the opposition of malaka and non-existence. And the way of reconciliation between his two statements, as indicated by the Imam, is that health with him is a disposition that is a principle for the soundness of actions, and at the time of disease that disposition ceases, and a disposition arises that is a principle for harm in actions. So if disease is made an expression for the non-existence of the first disposition and its ceasing, then between them is the opposition of non-existence and malaka. And if it is made an expression for the second disposition itself, then it is the opposition of contrariety. And it is as if he means that the word 'disease' is shared between the two matters, or is literal in one of them and metaphorical in the other, otherwise the problem remains. And it is said that the intended meaning is that between them is the opposition of non-existence and malaka according to investigation, which is the specific convention as has passed, or the opposition of contrariety according to common usage, which is the general convention, because the well-known is that two contraries are two matters attributed to one subject and cannot coexist, like evenness and oddness, not according to investigation so that they would be existing in the utmost difference under one proximate genus, and it has been clarified thus.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage defines disease according to Ibn Sina as a disposition contrary to health, causing unsound actions. It then notes a statement in al-Shifa where disease as such is pure non-existence (not bad temper or pain), suggesting opposition of privation and possession. The author reconciles these: health is a principle of sound actions; disease removes that and introduces a new principle of harm. The term 'disease' can refer either to the absence of health (privative opposition) or to the harmful state itself (contrary opposition). The passage explains that this ambiguity is either a shared word or literal/metaphorical use. Finally, it notes that according to technical investigation, the opposition is privative, while in common usage it is of contrariety, using the example of even and odd numbers.

Key Terms
مرض
هيئة مضادة
عدم محض
تقابل الملكة والعدم
تقابل التضاد
عرف خاص
عرف عام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Health and disease are simply contraries, like hot and cold.
Correction: In technical reality, their opposition is privative (possession vs. absence), not contrary. Disease is the absence of the health disposition, not a positive contrary state.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between health and disease in technical reality?
A) They are contraries.
B) They are contradictory.
C) They are in opposition of malaka and privation.
D) They are identical.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's analysis shows that the logical relationship between health and disease depends on whether disease is understood as the absence of health or the presence of a harmful state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the distinction between disease as a mere absence versus an active harmful state reshape the way you view your own spiritual ailments? Are you prone to seeing sins simply as lacks of virtue, or do you acknowledge them as powerful destructive states that require active counteraction? What specific change in your self-assessment is needed today?
?
Ibn Sina's analysis shows that the same word can hide a fundamental ambiguity. In your own life, where might you be using a single label (e.g., 'weakness', 'failure') and missing the deeper reality that determines whether you treat it as a privation to be filled or a positive illness to be cured? How will you investigate that distinction this week?
?
The reconciliation of Ibn Sina's definitions required seeing the same thing from two perspectives. What aspect of your faith are you currently viewing from only one angle, causing confusion or contradiction? What would it look like to hold both perspectives together, and what would that demand of you?

8 min
Disease as a Psychological Quality
Critique of disease definition and classification as psychological quality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that disease is defined as a psychological quality arising from specific bodily conditions, not merely as a physical deviation, and why the classification of disease into three types is loose.

Core Idea
Disease is a psychological quality, not just a physical state.

This passage opens a complex discussion by critiquing a prior definition of disease. The author argues that describing disease as 'bad composition' is insufficient unless properly qualified. He then explains that the standard division of disease into three categories is only a loose approximation, because disease is fundamentally a psychological quality that can manifest in various ways. Understanding this sets the stage for the later debate about an intermediate state between health and disease.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique of the definition
Read the first part of the passage where the author critiques an earlier definition of disease as an abbreviation. Notice how he argues that 'bad composition' is ambiguous unless specified.
The author calls the previous definition 'a defective abbreviation'.
2
Identify the classification of disease
Find where the author mentions the division of disease into bad temperament, bad composition, and discontinuity. Note that he calls this division 'a looseness'.
These three categories are commonly listed in medical texts.
3
Understand disease as a psychological quality
The author clarifies that the real meaning of disease is a psychological quality (كيفية نفسانية) that arises from these physical conditions. This is the key insight.
The classification is a means to conceptualize the underlying quality.
4
Connect to health analogy
Notice the analogy with health: just as health is applied to balanced temperament, disease is applied to the psychological state arising from imbalances.
This sets up the discussion of intermediate states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو الوضع أو عدم التركيب، فإنه اختصار مخل، والعذر بأنه لم يقيد بباقي المحتملات لظهور بطلانها ظاهر البطلان، لأن قولنا سوء التركيب إما مقدار يخل بالأفعال، أو عدد أو وضع أو انسداد مجرى كذلك ليس بياناً للمحتملات، بل للانقسام فليفهم، وتقرير الجواب بعد تسليم كون التضاد حقيقياً أن تقسيم المرض إلى سوء المزاج، وسوء التركيب وتفرق الاتصال تسامح، والمقصود أنه كيفية نفسانية تحصل عند هذه الأمور وتنقسم باعتبارها وهذا ما قيل إنها منوعات أطلق عليها اسم الأنواع، وذلك كما يطلق الصحة على اعتدال المزاج، أو المزاج المعتدل أنه من المحسوسات. ثم المعتبر في المرض إن كان يثبت الواسطة وإن كان آفة الجميع يثبت. قد اختلفوا في ثبوت الواسطة بين الصحة والمرض، وليس الخلاف في ثبوت حالة وصفة لا يصدق عليها الصحة ولا المرض كالعلم والقدرة والحياة إلى غير ذلك مما لا يحصى بل في ثبوت حالة لا يصدق معها على البدن أنه صحيح أو مريض، بل يصدق عليه أنه ليس بصحيح ولا مريض فأثبتها جالينوس كما للناقهين والمشايخ والأطفال، ومن ببعض أعضائه آفة دون البعض. ورد عليه ابن سينا بأنه أهمل الشرائط التي يجب أن تراعى حال ماله وسط، وما ليس له وسط، وهي أن يفرض الموضوع واحداً بعينه زمان واحد بعينه، وأن يكون الجزء واحداً بعينه والجهة والاعتبار واحدة بعينها فان فرض إنسان واحد، واعتبر منه عضو واحد أو أعضاء مختلفة معينة في زمان واحد وجاز أن لا يكون معتدل المزاج سوى التركيب، بحيث يصدر عنه الأفعال التي يتم بذلك العضو أو الأعضاء سليمة، وأن لا يكون ليس جميع كذلك، فهناك واسطة، وإن كان لا بد من أن يكون معتدل المزاج سوى التركيب، أو لا يكون معتدل المزاج سوى التركيب إما لثبوت أحدهما دون الآخر، أو لانتفائها جميعاً، فليس بينهما واسطة هذا كلامه، وقد اعتبر في المرض أن لا يكون جميع أفعال العضو سليمة، إما لكونه عبارة عن عدم الصحة التي هي مبدأ سلامة جميع الأفعال، أو عن هيئة بها يكون شيء من الأفعال مألوفاً، ولا خفاء في انتفاء الواسطة حينئذ، وأما إذا اعتبر في المرض الأفعال غير سليمة بأن يجعل عبارة عن هيئة بها يكون جميع أن يكون جميع أفعال العضو أعني الطبيعية والحيوانية والنفسانية مألوفة، فلا خفاء في ثبوت الواسطة بأن يكون بعض أفعال العضو سليماً دون البعض، وإن اعتبر آفة أفعال الأعضاء فثبوت الواسطة أظهر، وعلى هذا يكون الاختلاف مبنياً على جميع الاختلاف في تفسير المرض، وكلام الإمام مشعر بابتنائه على الاختلاف في تفسيرهما حيث قال يشبه أن يكون النزاع لفظياً فمن نفى الواسطة أراد بالصحة كون العضو الواحد، أو الأعضاء الكثيرة في الوقت الواحد أو في الأوقات الكثيرة بحيث يصدر عنه الأفعال سليمة وبالمرض أن لا يكون كذلك، ومن أثبتها أراد بالصحة كون كل الأعضاء بحيث يصدر عنها الأفعال تكون أفعالها سليمة، وبالمرض كون كل الأعضاء بحيث تكون أفعالها مألوفة وفي كلام ابن سينا ما يشعر بابتنائه على الاختلاف في تفسير الصحة حيث ذكر في أول القانون أنه لا تثبت الحالة الثالثة إلا أن تحدوا الصحة كما يشتهون
Or position or lack of composition, for it is a defective abbreviation, and the excuse that it was not restricted to the remaining possibilities due to the obviousness of their invalidity is clearly invalid, because our saying 'bad composition' is either an amount that disrupts actions, or number, or position, or obstruction of a passage likewise is not an explanation of the possibilities, but of the division, so understand. And the formulation of the answer after conceding that the contrariety is real is that the division of disease into bad temperament, bad composition, and discontinuity is a looseness, and the intended meaning is that it is a psychological quality that arises upon these matters and is divided according to them, and this is what was said: they are types given the name of species, just as health is applied to balanced temperament, or balanced temperament is from the perceptibles. Then what is considered in disease: if it establishes the intermediate state, and if it is a defect of the whole, it establishes it. They differed over the establishment of the intermediate state between health and disease, and the disagreement is not over the establishment of a state and attribute that is not called health nor disease, like knowledge, power, and life, and so forth without limit, but over the establishment of a state such that with it the body is not called healthy or diseased, rather it is called not healthy and not diseased. Galen affirmed it, as for convalescents, old men, and children, and for one who has a defect in some of his members but not others. Ibn Sina objected to him that he neglected the conditions that must be observed for what has an intermediate and what does not have an intermediate, which are: that the subject be assumed as one and the same, a single time, and that the part be one and the same, and the aspect and consideration be one and the same. For if one human is assumed, and one member or different specific members are considered in one time, and it is possible that he is not of balanced temperament except in composition, such that the actions that are completed by that member or members issue soundly, and that he is not all likewise, then there is an intermediate. And if it must be that he is balanced in temperament except in composition, or he is not balanced in temperament except in composition, either due to the establishment of one without the other, or due to their both being absent, then there is no intermediate between them. This is his speech. And he considered in disease that not all actions of the member be sound, either because it is an expression of the absence of health which is the principle of soundness of all actions, or of a state by which some of the actions are impaired. And there is no hiddenness in the negation of the intermediate then. But if he considered in disease the actions not sound, by making it an expression of a state by which all the actions of the member—namely natural, animal, and psychic—are impaired, then there is no hiddenness in the establishment of the intermediate, by some actions of the member being sound and others not. And if he considered a defect of the actions of members, then the establishment of the intermediate is more apparent. And upon this, the disagreement is based on the entire disagreement in the interpretation of disease. And the speech of the Imam indicates that it is based on the disagreement in their interpretation, for he said: 'It is likely that the dispute is verbal. For the one who negated the intermediate meant by health the single member, or many members, in one time or many times, such that actions issue from it soundly, and by disease that it is not like that. And the one who affirmed it meant by health all members such that actions issue from them—their actions are sound—and by disease all members such that their actions are impaired.' And in Ibn Sina's speech there is an indication that it is based on the disagreement in the interpretation of health, for he mentioned at the beginning of the Canon that the third state is not established unless you define health as you wish.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is a sophisticated discussion about the existence of an intermediate state between health and disease. It begins by critiquing an earlier definition of disease as an abbreviation that fails to list all possibilities, clarifying that the classification of disease into bad temperament, bad composition, and discontinuity is a loose classification. The real intended meaning is that disease is a psychological quality (كيفية نفسانية) that arises from these conditions. The author then moves to the main issue: whether there is a state that is neither health nor disease. The disagreement is not about states like knowledge or power, but about a bodily state where the body is not called healthy or diseased. Galen affirmed such a state, citing convalescents, the elderly, children, and those with partial defects. Ibn Sina objected, arguing that Galen neglected necessary conditions: the subject, time, part, aspect, and consideration must be fixed. Ibn Sina's analysis shows that if these conditions are observed, whether an intermediate exists depends on how health and disease are defined. If health is strict normality and disease is any deviation, there is no intermediate; but if disease is defined as all actions being impaired, then partial impairment yields an intermediate. The author then connects this to the Imam's (al-Razi's) view that the dispute is verbal: those who deny the intermediate define health and disease in a binary manner, while those who affirm it define them collectively. Finally, Ibn Sina's Canon is cited, indicating that the third state (i.e., the intermediate) is not established unless one defines health arbitrarily. The passage thus resolves the disagreement as a matter of definitional choice, not a factual dispute.

Key Terms
سوء المزاج
سوء التركيب
تفرق الاتصال
كيفية نفسانية
تسامح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Disease is merely a physical deviation in the body.
Correction: The author argues that disease is fundamentally a psychological quality that arises from physical conditions, not the physical conditions themselves.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the division of disease into three categories considered 'loose' (tasāmuḥ)?
A) Because the categories overlap
B) Because disease is actually a psychological quality, and these are just types
C) Because the categories are not exhaustive
D) Because the categories are not empirically observable

Key Takeaway
Disease is a psychological quality that arises from physical imbalances; the common classification is only a loose approximation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fall physically ill, do you ever pause to examine the psychological state that accompanies it—such as anxiety, impatience, or despair? How might recognizing disease as a psychological quality change the way you respond to your body's signals? Will you next time intentionally observe your inner state alongside your physical symptoms?
?
The author describes the common classification of disease as 'loose'. In your own life, do you tend to label your ailments rigidly, perhaps reducing them to mere diagnoses while ignoring the deeper reality? What would it mean for you to see your health challenges not just as problems to fix but as mirrors reflecting your inner condition? Are you willing to look beyond the surface?
?
Health is likened to balanced temperament. Consider your current life: is there true balance in your body, mind, and soul, or are you in a state of disease in some area? This lesson challenges you to see that your physical state echoes your soul's state. What concrete step will you take today to restore harmony, knowing that the root may be psychological? Will you commit to that step now?

intermediate

The Intermediate State: Dispute and Resolution
Dispute over an intermediate bodily state and its resolution as a verbal disagreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement between Galen and Ibn Sina about whether there is a state neither healthy nor diseased, and how the dispute is resolved by recognizing that it hinges on the definition of health and disease.

Core Idea
The existence of an intermediate state depends on how you define health and disease.

This passage delves into the heart of the debate: is there a bodily state that is neither healthy nor diseased? Galen thought so, citing examples like convalescents and the elderly. Ibn Sina objected, arguing that Galen failed to apply the necessary conditions for determining intermediacy. The discussion reveals that the answer depends on one's definition of health and disease, leading the Imam al-Razi to conclude that the dispute is verbal. Understanding this helps us see how definitions shape theological and medical conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question and Galen's position
Read the first part of the passage where it states that the disagreement is over a state that makes the body neither healthy nor diseased. Note Galen's examples: convalescents, elderly, children, and those with partial defects.
Galen affirmed the intermediate state.
2
Understand Ibn Sina's objection and conditions
Find where Ibn Sina objects and lists the conditions: subject, time, part, aspect must be fixed. He argues that under these conditions, either an intermediate exists or not depending on the specific scenario.
The conditions are crucial for analyzing intermediacy.
3
Analyze the two scenarios for the existence of an intermediate
The passage explains that if health is defined as all actions sound and disease as any deviation, then no intermediate. If disease is defined as all actions impaired, then partial impairment yields an intermediate.
The analysis shows the dispute is about definitions.
4
Recognize the verbal nature of the dispute
The author quotes al-Razi saying the dispute is likely verbal: those who deny the intermediate define health and disease for a single member, while those who affirm define them for all members collectively. Ibn Sina's Canon also hints at this.
The resolution is that there is no factual disagreement.

Emotional Qualities and the Movement of the Spirit
Al-Taftazani defines six emotional qualities by the movement of the spirit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Taftazani defines six emotional qualities based on the direction of the spirit's movement.

Core Idea
Emotions are not just feelings; they are movements of the spirit.

Al-Taftazani presents a fascinating physiological model of emotions, linking psychic states to the movement of the spirit (ruh) within the body. He defines six common emotions—joy, grief, anger, fright, sorrow, and worry—by whether the spirit moves outward, inward, or both. This passage shows how classical Islamic scholars integrated psychology and physiology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the six emotions
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the six emotions listed: joy (farah), grief (ghamm), anger (ghadab), fright (faza'), sorrow (huzn), worry (hamm).
Each emotion is defined in a similar structure.
2
Observe the common definition pattern
Each emotion is defined as a psychic quality followed by a movement of the spirit (ruh) either outward (kharij), inward (dakhil), or both. Note the specific reason for the movement.
3
Classify the emotions by direction of spirit movement
Group the emotions: outward (joy, anger), inward (grief, fright, sorrow), both (worry). Discuss why each direction fits the emotion's purpose.
Joy seeks pleasure outward; anger seeks revenge outward. Grief fears harm inward; fright flees harm inward; sorrow contracts slowly inward. Worry combines hope (outward) and fear (inward).
4
Reflect on the composition of worry
Notice that worry is uniquely described as composed of hope and fear, with the dominant one prevailing. This shows a more complex emotional state.
This is the only emotion defined as a mixture of two underlying forces.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكيفيات النابعة من انفعالات النفس
قد تعرض للنفس كيفيات نابعة لانفعالات تحدث فيها لما يرتسم في بعض قواها من النافع والضار كالفرح وهو كيفية نفسانية تتبعها حركة الروح إلى خارج البدن، طلبا للوصول إلى الملذ والغم وهو ما يتبعها حركة الروح إلى الداخل خوفاً من مؤذ واقع والغضب وهو ما يتبعها حركة الروح إلى الخارج طلباً للانتقام، والفزع وهو ما يتبعها حركة الروح إلى الداخل هرباً من المؤذي واقعاً كان أو متخيلاً، والحزن وهو ما يتبعها حركة الروح إلى الداخل قليلاً قليلاً، والهم وهو ما يتبعها حركة الروح إلى الداخل والخارج لحدوث أمر يتصور منه يقع أو شر ينتظر ، فهو مركب من جاء وخوف (۳) فأيهما غلب
Qualities arising from emotions of the soul. The soul is subject to qualities arising from emotions that occur in it due to what is perceived in some of its faculties as beneficial or harmful, such as joy (farah), which is a psychic quality followed by movement of the spirit (ruh) towards the outside of the body, seeking to reach the pleasurable; and grief (ghamm), which is followed by movement of the spirit to the inside, fearing a present harm; and anger (ghadab), which is followed by movement of the spirit to the outside, seeking revenge; and fright (faza'), which is followed by movement of the spirit to the inside, fleeing from a harm, whether actual or imagined; and sorrow (huzn), which is followed by movement of the spirit to the inside little by little; and worry (hamm), which is followed by movement of the spirit to the inside and outside due to the occurrence of something from which benefit is anticipated or evil is awaited. It is composed of hope and fear (3); whichever dominates prevails.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition of emotional qualities (kayfiyyat nabi'a min infi'alat al-nafs). Al-Taftazani explains that these are psychic qualities that arise from emotions caused by the soul's perception of benefit or harm through its faculties. He defines six specific emotions based on the direction of movement of the spirit (ruh): joy (farah) involves the spirit moving outward to attain pleasure; grief (ghamm) involves the spirit moving inward due to fear of actual harm; anger (ghadab) involves the spirit moving outward to seek revenge; fright (faza') involves the spirit moving inward to flee from harm (actual or imagined); sorrow (huzn) involves the spirit moving inward gradually; worry (hamm) involves the spirit moving both inward and outward because it involves anticipation of benefit or fear of evil, and it is composed of hope and fear, with the dominant one determining the overall state. The definitions are concise and focus on the bodily response (movement of the spirit) as a marker of the psychic state.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That these emotions are purely psychological without physical correlates.
Correction: The passage emphasizes the movement of the spirit (ruh) as a physical correlate, showing that emotions involve bodily responses.

Checkpoint

B) Sorrow (huzn)

Key Takeaway
Emotions in this framework are defined by observable physical responses (spirit movement) to perceived benefit or harm.

The Four Conditions for Perfect Health
Enumerates the four conditions for perfect health.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four necessary conditions that define perfect health according to classical medical theory.

Core Idea
Health is not just absence of disease—it requires soundness of all actions in every limb at all times.

This passage from a classical medical-theological text breaks down the concept of health into specific, rigorous conditions. Before we can talk about sickness or its causes, we need a precise definition of what it means to be truly healthy. The author enumerates four conditions that together delineate perfect health.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the enumerative structure: the author lists conditions using 'to exclude' (لتخرج).
2
Identify each condition
The passage presents four conditions for health. Find each one and understand what it excludes.
The conditions are: soundness of all actions, soundness of every limb, consistency in all times, and no near readiness for loss.
3
Reflect on why each condition is necessary
Ask yourself why a person with only partial soundness or seasonal health is not considered perfectly healthy. The commentary will help explain.
Each condition removes a specific imperfection.
4
Consider the common misconception
Think about how this definition differs from simply equating health with absence of disease.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جميع ويشترطوا شروطاً ما بهم إليها حاجة، وذلك مثل اشتراط سلامة الأفعال (۱) لتخرج صحة من يصدر عنه بعض الأفعال سليماً دون البعض ومن كل عضو، ولتخرج صحة من بعض أعضائه صحيح دون البعض، وفي كل وقت لتخرج صحة شتاء، ويمرض صيفاً، ومن غير استعداد قريب لزوالها لتخرج من يصح المشايخ والأطفال والناقهين.
All of them stipulate conditions that they need, such as stipulating the soundness of actions to exclude the health of one from whom some actions issue soundly without the others, and from every limb; and to exclude the health of one some of whose limbs are sound without the others; and at every time to exclude health in winter and illness in summer; and without a near readiness for its loss to exclude the health of elders, children, and convalescents.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage defines health (الصحة) by enumerating its necessary conditions for validity. The author explains that scholars of medicine stipulate conditions for health that they require. The first condition is the soundness of all actions (سلامة الأفعال): a person is not considered completely healthy if only some actions are sound while others are defective. Similarly, they require soundness from every limb (ومن كل عضو): health is not complete if only some limbs are sound. The third condition is at every time (وفي كل وقت): health must be consistent; someone who is healthy in winter but falls ill in summer is not considered fully healthy. The fourth condition is without a near readiness for its loss (من غير استعداد قريب لزوالها): health must not be fragile, as in the case of elders, children, and convalescents who are prone to quick loss of health. These conditions delineate the concept of perfect health.

سلامة الأفعال
مرض
استعداد قريب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Health is simply the absence of disease or illness.
Correction: Health requires positive conditions: all actions sound, all limbs sound, at all times, and not easily lost.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a condition for health?
A) Soundness of all actions
B) Soundness of every limb
C) Being healthy only in winter
D) No near readiness for loss of health

Key Takeaway
Perfect health is defined by four rigorous conditions: soundness of all actions and every limb, consistency across time, and robust stability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
